God's Purpose Is for Others, Not Himself
Refuting the claim that God acting for a purpose makes Him like creatures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's purpose in acting must be for His servants' benefit, not for Himself, and how the example of eternal punishment defends this position.

Core Idea
God's actions are for others' benefit, not His own.

This passage addresses a key objection: if God acts for a purpose, doesn't that make Him like creatures who act for their own benefit? The author explains that God is beyond needing benefit, so His purpose must be for others. He then tackles a subtle objection about whether God gains anything from benefiting others, using the stark example of eternal punishment to show that not all divine actions serve a beneficial purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the argument's two parts: first, establishing that God's purpose is for others; second, addressing an objection about benefit.
Pay attention to the shift at 'قلنا: نفع غيره...' which introduces the objection and response.
2
Identify the core claims
Identify the claim that God's purpose cannot be for Himself because He does not seek benefit. Then note how the author defines God's purpose as beneficence to servants.
3
Analyze the objection and counterexample
Follow the objection: if benefiting others is better for God than not, then God gains. Then see how the author counters by citing eternal punishment, which benefits no one.
This shows that not all God's actions have a purpose beneficial to others.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this argument preserves divine transcendence while acknowledging that God acts for reasons. Why is it important that God does not benefit from His own actions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فليس يلزم من كونه تعالى فاعلاً لغرض أن يكون من قبيل الأول إذ ليس كل من يفعل لغرض يفعل لغرض نفسه بل ذلك في حقه تعالى محال لتعاليه عن التضرر والانتفاع فتعين أن يكون غرضه راجعاً إلى عباده وهو الإحسان إليهم، بتحصيل مصالحهم ودفع مفاسدهم، ولا محذور في ذلك. قلنا: نفع غيره والإحسان إليه إن كان أولى بالنسبة إليه تعالى من عدمه جاء الإلزام لأنه تعالى يستفيد حينئذ بذلك النفع والإحسان ما هو أولى به وأصلح له وإلا لم يصلح أن يكون غرضاً له لما مر من العلم الضروري بذلك بل نقول: كيف ندعي وجوب تعليل أفعاله تعالى بمنافع العباد وأنا نعلم أن خلود أهل النار في النار من فعل الله ولا نفع فيه لهم ولا لغيرهم ضرورة.
It does not follow from His being, exalted, an agent for a purpose that He belongs to the category of the first [creatures], since not everyone who acts for a purpose acts for his own purpose; rather that is impossible in His case, exalted, because He is exalted above being harmed or benefiting. So it is determined that His purpose refers back to His servants, and it is beneficence to them, by achieving their interests and repelling their harms, and there is no objection in that. We say: benefiting another and being good to him, if it is better in relation to Him, exalted, than its non-existence, then the entailment [of necessitating purpose] comes about, because He then derives from that benefit and goodness what is better and more fitting for Him. Otherwise, if it is not better, it is not fit to be a purpose for Him due to the necessary knowledge that has passed of that. Rather we say: How can we claim the obligation of explaining His, exalted, actions by benefits to servants, while we know that the eternal punishment of the people of the Fire is an act of God, and there is no benefit in it for them or for others, necessarily.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a defense against the objection that if God acts for a purpose, He would be like created beings who act for their own benefit. The author argues that God is exalted above needing benefit or avoiding harm, so His purpose must be for His servants' benefit (إحسان). He then addresses a potential objection: if that benefit is better for God than its absence, then it would imply that God gains something, which is impossible. The response uses the example of the eternal punishment of hellfire, which benefits no one, to show that not all of God's actions have a purpose benefiting others, thus the claim that every action must have a purpose is false.

Key Terms
غرض
الإحسان
تعليل الأفعال
خلود أهل النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's purpose for others implies He benefits from their benefit.
Correction: God is exalted above needing any benefit. His actions for others do not bring Him advantage, as shown by the example of eternal punishment, which benefits no one.

Checkpoint
What point does the example of the eternal punishment of hellfire illustrate?
A) Some of God's actions have no purpose or benefit for anyone.
B) God always acts for the benefit of His servants.
C) Punishment is actually beneficial for the punished.
D) God's actions are all purposeless.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not for His own benefit but for His servants' benefit, yet not all actions benefit others, as shown by eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or seek God's help, do you assume He is obligated to benefit you—as if He were a creature who needs to please you? How does this lesson's insistence that God's purpose is for others, yet not for His own need, challenge that assumption?
?
Think of a hardship or punishment you have witnessed that seemed to have no redemptive value. Have you ever doubted God's goodness because you could not see the benefit? How does the example of eternal punishment in this passage reshape your trust in God's actions, even when they seem purposeless from your perspective?
?
If God's goodness includes actions that bring no benefit to anyone, do you still believe He is good? What does this lesson compel you to say to yourself the next time you question a divine decree that brings you harm?

Why God's Acts Cannot Have External Purposes
Sanusi argues that external purposes are impossible for God, defining purpose and showing its inapplicability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's acts cannot have external purposes and how the directness of divine creation rules out any causal intermediary.

Core Idea
God creates all things directly, so nothing can serve as a purpose for another act.

In the previous lesson, we saw one proof against purposes in God's acts. Here, Sanusi presents a second, even more direct argument: the very definition of a purpose involves an external thing achieved through the act's mediation. Since God creates everything directly without intermediaries, no such thing can exist for Him. This passage demolishes the idea that one divine act could be a means to another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the argument unfold naturally before analyzing it.
Notice how Sanusi defines 'purpose' before applying it to God.
2
Understand the definition of 'purpose'
The passage defines a purpose as something external that comes about through the act's mediation. Identify this definition and see why it is key to the argument.
If an act has no role in producing something, that something cannot be its purpose.
3
See why this fails for God
Since God creates all things directly and independently, no act serves as a causal intermediary for another. Hence, nothing can be a purpose of any divine act.
This is the core of the proof: direct creation eliminates all causal links between acts.
4
Reflect on the arbitrariness argument
If no act has a causal entry into another, then choosing one as a purpose over another is completely arbitrary. This shows that any attempt to assign purposes is baseless.
This highlights the absurdity of applying human-like purposeful action to God.
5
Consider the broader implication
The passage concludes that no explanation by purposes (ta'lil) is conceivable in God's acts at all. This reinforces the principle that God's actions are beyond human reasoning about ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما أي ثاني الوجهين أن غرض الفعل أمر خارج عنه يحصل تبعاً للفعل وبتوسطه أي يكون للفعل مدخل في وجوده وهذا مما لا يتصور في أفعاله إذ هو تعالى فاعل لجميع الأشياء ابتداء كما بيناه فيما سلف فلا يكون شيء من الكائنات والحوادث إلا فعلا له صادراً بتأثير قدرته فيه ابتداءً بلا واسطة لا غرضاً لفعل آخر له مدخل في وجوده بحيث لا يحصل ذلك الشيء إلا به ليصلح أن يكون غرضاً لذلك الفعل حاصلاً بتوسطه وليس جعل البعض من أفعاله وآثاره غرضاً أولى من البعض الآخر إذ لا مدخل لشيء منها في وجود الآخر على تقدير استنادها بأسرها إليه على سواء، فجعل بعضها غرضاً من بعض آخر دون عكسه تحكم بحت فلا يتصور تعليل في أفعاله أصلاً.
And the second [of the two proofs] is that the purpose of an act is something external to it that occurs consequent upon the act and through its mediation, meaning that the act has an entry into its existence. And this is inconceivable in His acts, since He, exalted, is the agent of all things directly, as we explained previously. So nothing among existents and events is except an act of His emanating from the effect of His power in it directly without intermediary, not a purpose for another act that has an entry into its existence such that that thing does not occur except through it to be fit as a purpose of that act occurring through its mediation. And making some of His acts and effects a purpose over others is not more appropriate, since none of them has an entry into the existence of another, on the assumption that they all depend on Him equally. So making some of them a purpose for others without the reverse is pure arbitrariness, so no explanation by purposes is conceivable in His acts at all.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against purpose-based explanation of God's actions. The author defines a purpose as something external to the act that is achieved through the act's mediation. Since God creates all things directly by His power without any intermediary, nothing can serve as a purpose for another act, because that would require the first act to contribute to the existence of the second. But all things depend equally on God, so no act has a causal role in another. Hence, selecting one as a purpose for another is arbitrary and impossible. Thus, the very concept of a purpose (غرض) is inapplicable to God's acts.

Key Terms
غرض الفعل
فاعل ابتداء
مدخل في الوجود
تحكم بحت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's actions can be compared to human actions where one act serves as a means to another.
Correction: God creates all things directly, so no act is a means to another. Therefore, the concept of a purpose that comes through an act's mediation is inapplicable.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that making one of God's acts a purpose for another is 'pure arbitrariness'?
A) Because all acts are equally direct creations of God.
B) Because God's acts are too many to prioritize.
C) Because purposes are not mentioned in revelation.
D) Because human reason cannot know God's purposes.

Key Takeaway
God's direct creation of all things eliminates any possibility of external purposes in His acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes assume that God's actions are like your own—aiming at outcomes you can see? How does this passage challenge that assumption? What specific attitude should change in your daily reliance on God?
?
If God's acts have no purposes external to them, then what does that mean for your understanding of trials and blessings? Are they not 'for' something you can grasp? How should this affect your patience and gratitude?
?
When you pray for something, do you treat the outcome as a 'purpose' that your prayer causes? Based on this lesson, how should you reframe your expectation of God's response?

The Infinite Regress Argument Against Purposes
Showing that requiring purposes leads to infinite regress unless a final purpose is allowed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why requiring a purpose for every act of God leads to an infinite regress and how the argument concludes that a final self-purposed act would have no purpose, thus refuting the necessity of purpose.

Core Idea
Purposes cannot go on forever; a final purpose has no purpose.

This passage presents a classic philosophical argument against the necessity of purposes in divine acts. It shows that if every act must have a purpose, an infinite chain results, which is absurd. To avoid this, one must allow an act that is its own purpose, but then that act lacks a purpose, undermining the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: if acts are explained by purposes, then...
The passage begins with 'وأيضاً' indicating it is part of a larger discussion.
2
Identify the two branches
The argument presents two possibilities: either an infinite regress of purposes or a final self-purposed act. Understand why both are problematic.
3
Analyze the contradiction
If a self-purposed act is accepted, it has no purpose, which violates the original assumption that every act must have a purpose.
4
Consider the objection
The passage mentions a potential objection about conceptual distinction. Reflect on why this does not resolve the contradiction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the argument invalidates the claim that every act of God must have a purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إذا عللت أفعاله بالأغراض فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى ما هو الغرض والمقصود في نفسه وإلا تسلسلت الأغراض إلى ما لا نهاية لها ولا يكون ذلك الذي هو غرض ومقصود في نفسه لغرض آخر لأنه خلاف ما فرض وإذا جاز ذلك بطل القول بوجوب الغرض إذ قد انتهى أفعاله إلى فعل لا غرض له وهو الذي كان مقصوداً في نفسه وقد يقال: لا يجب في الغرض كونه مغايرا بالذات، بل يكفيه التغاير الاعتباري.
And also, if His acts are explained by purposes, then it must end at what is the purpose and intended in itself, otherwise the purposes would be infinite. And that which is a purpose and intended in itself would not be for another purpose because that contradicts what was assumed. And if that is allowed, then the statement of the obligation of a purpose is invalidated, since His acts would have ended at an act that has no purpose, namely that which was intended in itself. And it may be said: it is not necessary for the purpose to be distinct in essence, rather the conceptual distinction suffices.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a regress argument. If every act of God requires a purpose, and each purpose is itself an act requiring a purpose, an infinite chain results. To avoid this, there must be a final purpose that is intended for itself, not for another purpose. But that final purpose then has no purpose, which contradicts the claim that every act must have a purpose. Thus, requiring purposes leads to a contradiction unless one accepts that some acts have no purpose. The author also notes a potential objection: the purpose might be conceptually distinct from the act even if identical in essence, but this is dismissed by implication since the point stands that the regress must stop.

Key Terms
تسلسل الأغراض
غرض مقصود في نفسه
تغاير اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infinite regress can be avoided by having all purposes be simultaneous or by distinguishing essence from concept.
Correction: The argument shows that even if purposes are simultaneous, the chain still requires a final self-purposed act that has no purpose, which contradicts the necessity of purpose. The conceptual distinction objection is noted but does not resolve the fundamental contradiction.

Checkpoint
What problem does the author identify with the claim that every act must have a purpose?
A) It leads to an infinite regress unless a self-purposed act is accepted, which itself lacks purpose.
B) It contradicts the oneness of God.
C) It requires that purposes be external to the act.
D) It implies that God is subject to external constraints.

Key Takeaway
Requiring a purpose for every act of God leads to a logical contradiction, so the necessity of purpose cannot be maintained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified your actions by an endless chain of reasons, avoiding the uncomfortable truth that some things you do are for their own sake? How does this argument push you to re-examine your own motives?
?
When you think about your worship, do you sometimes feel it must have a purpose to be valid? What if the worship itself—without any further purpose—is what matters? Can you accept an act of devotion that is its own purpose?
?
This lesson argues that requiring a purpose for every act leads to a contradiction. In your life, where do you see yourself trapped in a similar mental loop—seeking reasons for everything, even when the reason is simply that it is good in itself? Will you commit to recognizing something today that is valuable without explanation?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Argument from Absurdity
Refuting the Mu'tazila claim that purposeless acts are absurd and must be avoided

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God must have a purpose in His actions by exposing the ambiguity of the term 'عبث'.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argument from absurdity collapses on its own undefined terms.

In this passage, the author tackles a key Mu'tazila proof: that an act without purpose is 'عبث' (absurd) and therefore evil, so God must avoid it. The response is a masterclass in conceptual clarity. By asking whether 'عبث' simply means 'without purpose' or something else, the author shows the argument is either circular or unsubstantiated. Let's see how this logical dismantling works.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument carefully
Identify their claim: that an act devoid of purpose is 'عبث' and evil, so God must have a purpose in His acts.
Notice that they assume their definition of 'عبث'.
2
Understand the first response – circularity
If 'عبث' simply means 'without purpose', then they are merely asserting what they need to prove. This is a circular argument.
The author points out this is 'first of the disputed matter'.
3
Learn the three requirements if they mean something else
They must: 1) define that other meaning (تصوير), 2) prove it applies to a purposeless act (تقرير), 3) prove such an act is impossible (دلالة على امتناع).
These are the logical burdens the Mu'tazila fail to meet.
4
Reflect on the significance
This refutation shows the importance of clear definitions and burden of proof in theological debate.
It also protects God's absolute freedom from being constrained by human notions of purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا أي المعتزلة على وجود الغرض في أفعاله تعالى بأن الفعل الخالي عن الغرض عبث وأنه قبيح بالضرورة يجب تنزيه الله عنه لكونه عالماً بقبحه واستغنائه عنه فلا بد إذن في فعله من غرض يعود إلى غيره نفيا للعبث والنقض. قلنا في جوابهم: إن أردتم بالعبث ما لا غرض فيه من الأفعال فهو أول المسألة المتنازع فيها إذ نحن نجوز أن يصدر عنه تعالى فعل لا غرض فيه أصلا وأنتم تمنعونه وتعبرون عنه بالعبث فلا يجديكم نفعاً، وإن أردتم بالعبث أمراً آخر فلا بد لكم أولاً: من تصويره، أي تصوير ذلك الأمر الآخر حتى نفهمه ونتصوره، ثم ثانياً: من تقريره أي بيانه ثبوت ذلك المفهوم للفعل على تقدير خلوه من الغرض، ثم ثالثاً: من الدلالة على امتناعه أي استحالة الفعل المتصف.
The Mu'tazila argued for the existence of purpose in His, exalted, acts on the grounds that an act devoid of purpose is absurd and that it is necessarily evil, and God must be exalted above it because He knows its evil and is independent of it. So necessarily in His act there must be a purpose returning to another, to negate absurdity and deficiency. We say in response to them: If you mean by 'absurd' what has no purpose among acts, then that is the very point of contention, for we allow that an act with no purpose at all may issue from Him, exalted, while you forbid it and call it 'absurd', so it does not benefit you. And if you mean by 'absurd' something else, then you must first present a conception of it, i.e., a depiction of that other thing so that we may understand and conceive it; then secondly, present a demonstration of it, i.e., a proof that that concept is realized in the act on the assumption of its being devoid of purpose; then thirdly, proof of its impossibility, i.e., the impossibility of the act characterized by it.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's argument that an act without purpose is 'عبث' (absurd/pointless) and evil, so God must avoid it. The author gives a three-step response. First, if 'عبث' simply means 'without purpose', then the argument is circular because that is exactly what is in dispute. Second, if they mean something else, they must define it clearly (تصوير). Third, they must prove that this concept applies to a purposeless act (تقرير). Fourth, they must prove that such an act is impossible (دلالة على امتناع). Since they fail to do so, their argument is ineffective.

Key Terms
عبث
قبيح
تصوير
تقرير
دلالة على امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'عبث' is self-evidently applicable to God's acts without further definition.
Correction: The author shows that 'عبث' requires clear definition and proof before it can be used to judge God's actions.

Checkpoint
What is the author's first response to the Mu'tazila's claim that purposeless acts are 'عبث'?
A) The term 'عبث' is ambiguous and needs clarification.
B) If 'عبث' means 'without purpose', then the argument is circular.
C) God's actions cannot be judged by human standards.
D) There is no consensus on the meaning of 'عبث'.

Key Takeaway
The author dismantles the Mu'tazila argument by showing that the term 'عبث' is either circularly defined or requires unprovided proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that the Mu'tazila argued that God must act with purpose, but the Ash'ari response shows that this argument collapses on its own undefined terms. When you face trials where God's purpose is hidden, do you trust that He is not 'عبث' even if you cannot conceive the reason? Where in your life are you demanding God explain His purpose before you submit?
?
The author insists that the Mu'tazila define 'عبث' before using it against God. In your own thoughts about God's actions—like why He allows suffering or why He commands certain things—are you using terms like 'fair' or 'good' without defining them by revelation? What would it cost you to let God define those terms for you?

The Purpose of Obligations and the Sultan Analogy
Objection and answer on the purpose of obligations with a sultan analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that the purpose of difficult obligations is to enable servants to earn reward with honor, and why giving reward without prior merit would be ugly.

Core Idea
God's obligations serve to make reward deserved, avoiding the ugliness of unearned honor.

This passage addresses a key objection: if God has purposes for His actions, then why impose difficult obligations that benefit neither Him nor us? The Ash'ari response uses a striking analogy of a sultan and a dustman to show that obligations are a means to grant us reward with honor, which would be unbecoming if given without preparation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get the full argument.
2
Identify the objection
What is the central question about the purpose of difficult obligations? Note that it comes from those who deny that God acts for purposes.
3
Understand the answer: reward with honor
The answer is that obligations expose the servant to reward, which is a perpetual benefit with honor. But such honor without prior merit is ugly by reason.
4
Study the sultan analogy
The sultan giving wealth to a dustman is generous, but if he then dismounts and has his hand kissed, it becomes ugly. How does this illustrate the necessity of merit?
5
Clarify the meaning of 'istihqaq' (merit)
The passage distinguishes two meanings: (1) natural ordering of reward upon deeds (undisputed), and (2) a necessary right that the giver is blamed for abandoning (denied).
Only the second meaning is relevant to the ugliness argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب : إذا قيل لهم : ( أنتم قد أوجبتم الغرض في أفعاله تعالى ( فما الغرض من هذه التكاليف الشاقة التي لا نفع فيها لله لتعاليه عنه ولا للعبد لأنها مشقة بلا حظ ؟ قالوا : الغرض فيها عائد إلى العباد وهو ) تعريض العبد للثواب ( في الدار الآخرة وتمكينه منه ) فإن الثواب تعظيم) أي منفعة دائمة مقرونة بتعظيم وإكرام (وهو) أي التعظيم المذكور ( بدون استحقاق سابق قبيح عقلاً . ألا يرى أن السلطان إذا أمر بزبال وأعطاه من المال ما لا يدخل تحت الحصر لم يستقبح منه أصلا بل عد جودا وفضلا وإغناء للفقير وتبعيداً له عن ساحة الهوان بالكلية لكنه مع ذلك إذا نزل له وقام بين يديه معظماً له ومكرماً إياه وأمر خدمه بتقبيل أنامله استقبح منه ذلك وذمه العقلاء ونسبوه إلى ركاكة العقل وقلة الدراية فالله سبحانه لما أراد أن يعطي عباده منافع دائمة مقرونة بإجلال وإكرام منه ومن ملائكته المقربين ولم يحسن أن يتفضل بذلك عليهم ابتداء بلا استحقاق كلفهم ما يستحقونه .به قوله : (بدون استحقاق سابق قبيح قالوا وللأعمال تأثير في الاستحقاق لقوله تعالى : جزاء بما كانوا يعملون ﴾ [السجدة : ۱۷] ، ونظائره وأنت خبير بأن الاستحقاق بمعنى ترتب الثواب والعقاب على الأعمال مما لا نزاع فيه وأما بمعنى كون ذلك الترتب حقاً لازماً بحيث يذم تاركه كما زعموه فممنوع ولا دليل في الكتاب عليه . قوله : ( ولم يحسن أن يتفضل بذلك عليهم ابتداء فيه بحث وهو أنه لو سلم قبح التفضل بالثواب ابتداء لم يندفع بالتكليف إذ لا نسبة لطاعة العبد ولو استغرق عمره ولم يعصه طرفة عين إلى ما وعده الله تعالى من الثواب بفضله وجوده ألا ترى أن الأمير إذا أمر الزبال بأن يزبل نقرة واقعة في الطريق ففعله ثم أعطاه كثيراً من المال وأجله غاية الإجلال فنزل له، ولو قام بين يديه معظماً له ومكرماً إياه وأمر خدمه بتقبيل أنامله يذم عند العقلاء أيضاً .
Appendix: When it is said to them: 'You have made purpose necessary in His actions, exalted—so what is the purpose of these difficult obligations which have no benefit for God, exalted above that, nor for the servant because it is hardship without gain?' They said: 'The purpose in them pertains to the servants, and it is exposing the servant to reward in the Hereafter and enabling him to obtain it. For reward is a glorification, i.e., a perpetual benefit accompanied by glorification and honor, and it, i.e., the mentioned glorification, without prior merit is ugly by reason. Do you not see that if a sultan commands a dustman and gives him wealth beyond count, it is not considered ugly from him at all; rather, it is considered munificence, virtue, enrichment of the poor, and complete removal of him from the domain of humiliation. But with that, if he dismounts for him, stands before him glorifying him and honoring him, and orders his servants to kiss his fingertips, that is considered ugly from him, and the intelligent blame him and attribute it to foolishness and lack of knowledge. Thus God, glorified, when He wanted to give His servants perpetual benefits accompanied by veneration and honor from Him and His angels, and it was not fitting that He bestow that on them initially without merit, He obligated them with what merits it.' His statement: 'without prior merit is ugly.' They said: 'And deeds have an effect on merit, according to His statement: "A reward for what they used to do" [Sajda 32:17] and its parallels. And you are aware that merit in the sense of the ordering of reward and punishment upon deeds is something about which there is no dispute. But in the sense of that ordering being a necessary right such that the one who abandons it is blamed—as they claim—it is denied, and there is no proof in the Book for it.' His statement: 'And it was not fitting that He bestow that on them initially' contains a discussion: even if one concedes the ugliness of bestowing reward initially, it is not removed by obligation, because there is no proportion between the obedience of a servant—even if he spends his whole life and never disobeys Him for the blink of an eye—and the reward that God has promised out of His bounty and generosity. Do you not see that if a prince commands a dustman to sweep a puddle in the road, and he does it, then the prince gives him much wealth and honors him with utmost veneration, dismounts for him, stands before him glorifying him and honoring him, and orders his servants to kiss his fingertips—he would also be blamed by the intelligent.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section (تذنيب). The opponent says: you (the Ash'aris) have made purpose necessary in God's actions, so what is the purpose of difficult obligations that benefit neither God nor the servant? The answer: the purpose is to expose the servant to reward, which is a permanent benefit with honor. But this reward without prior merit is deemed ugly by reason. An analogy is given: a sultan giving wealth to a dustman is not ugly, but if he then honors him excessively (standing, bowing, kissing fingertips), it becomes ugly. Similarly, God wants to give honor, but to avoid ugliness, He makes the servant earn it through obligations. Then a comment on 'بدون استحقاق سابق قبيح' discusses the meaning of 'استحقاق' (merit). It clarifies that merit in the sense of the natural ordering of reward upon deeds is undisputed, but in the sense of a necessary right that one is blamed for abandoning, it is denied. Then another comment on 'ولم يحسن أن يتفضل' argues that even if bestowing reward initially were ugly, the obligations do not solve the problem because the servant's obedience is incomparable to the promised reward, using another sultan analogy.

Key Terms
تذنيب
التكاليف الشاقة
تعريض العبد للثواب
استحقاق
الفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward without prior merit is necessarily ugly in all cases.
Correction: The passage shows it is only ugly when the reward is excessive honor; giving wealth without merit is not ugly.

Checkpoint
According to the sultan analogy, why is it considered ugly for the sultan to have the dustman's hand kissed?
A) Because the dustman is unclean
B) Because it is excessive honor without prior deserving
C) Because the dustman did not earn it
D) Because the sultan is too generous

Key Takeaway
God imposes obligations to make reward deserved, thus avoiding the rational ugliness of unearned honor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you view them as burdensome tasks or as opportunities to earn honor from God? What does your attitude reveal about your understanding of His generosity?
?
If you truly believed that your good deeds are not a ‘right’ upon God but solely His grace to grant reward, how would that change the way you pray, fast, or give charity? Are you willing to let go of the sense of entitlement?
?
The sultan gave wealth freely but considered honor to require merit. When you ask God for honor in the Hereafter, are you expecting it as a gift or as something you’ve earned? Which expectation is more humbling, and which is more motivating?

6 min
The Proportion Problem: Can Obedience Earn Such Reward?
Further objection on the inadequacy of obligations and a second analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that even with obligations, the servant's deeds are disproportionate to the promised reward, and the counter-analogy of the prince and dustman.

Core Idea
No amount of obedience can truly earn God's immense reward, yet He still uses obligations as a means of granting it.

Even if we accept that reward without prior merit is ugly, does imposing obligations solve the problem? The commentator argues that the servant's obedience is utterly disproportionate to the reward, making the second sultan analogy show that even with some effort, excessive honor remains ugly. This deepens our understanding of divine generosity and the role of obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read this short but dense passage carefully. It is a further objection to the previous answer.
2
Understand the new objection
Even if bestowing reward initially is ugly, why would obligations fix it? The servant's obedience is meager compared to the immense reward.
3
Study the second sultan analogy
A prince commands a dustman to sweep a puddle (small task), then gives him wealth and excessive honor. He is still blamed. Why?
The task is too minor to warrant such honor.
4
Reflect on the implication
The analogy shows that even with some effort, if the reward far exceeds the effort, the giver still appears foolish. Thus God's reward remains gracious.

According to the second sultan analogy, why would the prince still be blamed even after the dustman performed the task?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the immense reward of Paradise is entirely unearned, even after a lifetime of obedience, does that make you feel more grateful or more anxious? Why?
?
The dustman in the analogy was overwhelmed by the prince's generosity. In your own spiritual life, do you truly feel overwhelmed by God's mercy, or do you still quietly believe you deserve something? What would change if you fully internalized that nothing can ever be earned?

The Objection Against Reward as the Purpose of Obligation
Argues against reward as purpose and concludes with Sharh al-Maqasid on wisdoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that obligation's exposure to punishment outweighs its exposure to reward, and the concluding affirmation from Sharh al-Maqasid that some acts are caused by wisdoms and interests.

Core Idea
Obligation's purpose is not solely reward because it also exposes to punishment, which outweighs the benefit.

In this passage, the argument that obligation is purely for exposure to reward is challenged. The author points out that obligation also exposes to punishment, and the majority experience punishment, making the benefit insufficient. A counter-answer is suggested, and then a quote from Sharh al-Maqasid affirms that some acts are indeed caused by wisdoms and interests, as supported by Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reasoning
Read the first part of the passage where the author argues against reward as the sole purpose of obligation. Notice how he compares the exposure to reward and punishment.
Pay attention to the phrase 'معارض بما فيه من تعريض الكافر والفاسق للعذاب'.
2
Understand the counter-answer
Read the marginal comment that offers a possible reply: exposure to reward with enabling is good, and punishment is due to bad choice.
This counter-answer defends the goodness of obligation despite the punishment.
3
Examine the concluding quote
Read the quote from Sharh al-Maqasid which states that some acts, especially legal rulings, are caused by wisdoms and interests, with Quranic evidence.
This shows that the purpose of obligations is not completely denied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه ) أي ما ذكرتم من أن التكليف تعريض للثواب ) معارض بما فيه من تعريض الكافر والفاسق للعذاب ) إذ لولا التكليف لم يستحقا عقاباً. ومن أين لكم أن ذلك التعريض للثواب (أكثر من هذا أي التعريض للعذاب ؟ بل نقول : إن الثاني أكثر من الأول لأن الغلبة للكفرة والفسقة وإذا لم تكن المنفعة أكثر من المضرة لم تصلح تلك المنفعة لأن تكون غرضاً للحكيم العالم بأحوال الأشياء كلها الآتي بالأفعال على وجهها، فبطل ما ذكرتموه من غرض التكليف . قوله : ( معارض بما فيه من تعريض الكافر قد يجاب عنه بأن التعريض للثواب مع التمكين المحسنة للتكليف ولا يبطل حسنه بتعذيب الكافر والفاسق من اكتساب السعادة الأبدية هي . لسوء اختيارهما. قوله: (فبطل ما ذكرتموه من عرض التكليف قال في شرح المقاصد : الحق تعليل بعض الأفعال سيما شرعية الأحكام بالحكم والمصالح ظاهر كإيجاب الحدود والكفارات وتحريم المسكرات وما أشبه ذلك والنصوص شاهدة بذلك كقوله تعالى : وما خلقت الجن والإنس الا ليعبدون ﴾ [الذاريات : ٥٦] ، الآية ومن أجل ذلك كتبنا على بني إسرائيل ﴾ [المائدة : ٣٢ ] ،
Then it—i.e., what you mentioned that obligation is exposure to reward—is opposed by what it contains of exposure of the disbeliever and the sinner to punishment, for without obligation they would not deserve punishment. And where do you get that this exposure to reward is greater than this exposure to punishment? Rather, we say that the second is greater than the first because the majority are disbelievers and sinners. And if the benefit is not greater than the harm, that benefit is not fit to be a purpose for the Wise One who knows the states of all things and performs actions in their proper manner. Thus, what you mentioned of the purpose of obligation is nullified. His statement: 'opposed by what it contains of exposure of the disbeliever...' It may be answered that exposure to reward, along with enabling, is the good aspect of obligation, and its goodness is not invalidated by the punishment of the disbeliever and sinner due to their own bad choice in attaining eternal happiness. His statement: 'Thus, what you mentioned of the purpose of obligation is nullified.' He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'The truth is that the causation of some acts, especially legal rulings, by wisdoms and interests is apparent, such as the imposition of prescribed punishments and expiations, the prohibition of intoxicants, and the like. Texts attest to this, such as His saying: "And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me" [Adh-Dhariyat 51:56] and "Because of that, We decreed upon the Children of Israel" [Al-Ma'idah 5:32].'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a further objection and a concluding remark. The author argues that the alleged purpose of obligations (exposure to reward) is opposed by the fact that obligations also expose people to punishment (for disbelievers and sinners). Without obligation, they would not deserve punishment. Moreover, the exposure to punishment is greater because the majority are disbelievers and sinners. Since the benefit (reward) is not greater than the harm (punishment), it cannot be a purpose for the Wise. Thus, the earlier claim is nullified. A marginal comment suggests a possible answer: the exposure to reward along with enabling is the good aspect, and the punishment is due to their own bad choice, so it does not invalidate the goodness. Another comment quotes Sharh al-Maqasid, stating that indeed some acts, especially legal rulings, are caused by wisdoms and interests, and the Quranic verses support this (creation for worship, and the decree for the Children of Israel). This serves as a final remark on the issue.

Key Terms
تعريض الكافر للعذاب
الغرض
غرض التكليف
شرح المقاصد
المصالح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the purpose of obligations is solely to provide reward, ignoring the punishment aspect.
Correction: The passage shows that obligation also exposes to punishment, which outweighs reward for the majority, so it cannot be the sole purpose. However, some obligations are still caused by wisdoms and interests.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why does the exposure to reward fail as a purpose for obligation?
A) Because reward is not given
B) Because the punishment exposure is greater in number
C) Because obligation is unnecessary
D) Because God does not act for purposes

Key Takeaway
The purpose of obligation is not simply reward; the objection based on punishment exposure is countered by the claim that goodness lies in enabling, and the final quote from Sharh al-Maqasid affirms that some acts are caused by wisdoms and interests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your obedience to Allah exposes you to the risk of punishment if you fail? How does this realization affect the sincerity and consistency of your worship today?
?
The passage says the majority of people are disbelievers and sinners. If you are honest, do you find yourself among that majority in some areas of your life? What obligation are you currently neglecting, and what would it take for you to fulfill it with the awareness that your choice determines eternal happiness or misery?
?
The quote from Sharh al-Maqasid connects legal rulings like prohibitions of intoxicants to worldly interests. Think of one sin you struggle with. Can you identify the worldly harm it causes that Allah in His wisdom has protected you from? Does that change how you view that obligation as a mercy rather than a burden?

The Fourfold Division of Divine Names
al-Jurjani presents the fourfold classification of names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four ways names are derived from a thing and how they apply to Allah.

Core Idea
All names for Allah come from four origins: essence, part, attribute, or action.

Before discussing specific divine names, the author establishes a classification of how names are derived. This passage introduces the four categories: names taken from the essence, a part, an external attribute, or an action. It then sets the stage for determining which are possible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the fourfold division introduced in the first paragraph.
The division is presented in a single condensed sentence.
2
Identify the four categories
Identify the four sources from which a name can be taken: essence, part, external attribute, action.
These are the basic building blocks for all names.
3
Observe the transition to application
Note how the author then says 'then we consider which of them is possible for Allah the Exalted', indicating that the classification is general and needs further scrutiny.
This transition sets up the detailed analysis in the following lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : في أقسام الاسم اللفظ ( في أقسام الاسم، اعلم أن الاسم الذي يطلق على الشيء (إما أن يؤخذ من الذات ) بأن يكون المسمى به ذات الشيء من حيث هو ( أو من جزئها أو من وصفها الخارجي أو من الفعل ) الصادر عنه فهذه هي أقسام الاسم على الإطلاق ( ثم ننظر أيها الله يمكن في حق تعالى، أما المأخوذ من الذات ففرع تعقلها، وقد تكلمنا فيه) فمن ذهب إلى جواز تعقل ذاته جوز أن يكون له اسم بإزاء حقيقته المخصوصة، ومن ذهب إلى امتناع تعقلها لم يجوز له اسماً مأخوذاً من ذاته لأن وضع الاسم لمعنى فرع ووسيلة إلى تفهيمه فإذا لم يمكن أن يعقل ويفهم فلا يتصور بإزائه وفيه بحث لأن الخلاف في تعقل كنه ذاته ووضع الاسم لا يتوقف عليه إذ يجوز أن يعقل ذات ما بوجه من وجوهه ويوضع الاسم لخصوصيته ويقصد تفهيمها باعتبار ما لا بكنهها، ويكون ذلك الوجه مصححاً للوضع وخارجاً عن مفهوم الاسم على ما مر من أن لفظ الله اسم علم له موضوع لذاته من غير اعتبار معنى فيه ( وأما المأخوذ من الجزء) كالجسم للإنسان مثلاً ( فمحال عليه ) تعالى ( لما بينا من أن الوجوب الذاتي ينافي التركيب ( فلا يتصور لذاته تعالى جزء حتى يطلق اسمه عليه ( وأما المأخوذ من الوصف (الخارجي) الداخل في مفهوم الاسم ( فجائز) في حقه تعالى ( ثم هذا الوصف قد يكون حقيقياً ) كالعليم ( وقد يكون إضافياً) كالماجد بمعنى المعالي ويكون سلبياً) كالقدوس، ( وأما المأخوذ من الفعل فجائز) في حقه تعالى أيضاً (فهذه الأقسام المذكورة للاسم هي ( أقسامه البسيطة وقد تتركب ثنائياً وأكثر، وستعلم أمثلتها فيما يتبعه من المقصد )
The second purpose: on the divisions of the name, the word. (On the divisions of the name: know that the noun which is applied to a thing (is either taken from the essence) such that the named is the essence of the thing as such, (or from its part, or from its external attribute, or from the action) emanating from it. These are the divisions of the name absolutely. (Then we consider which of them is possible for Allah the Exalted. As for that taken from the essence, it is a branch of conceiving it, and we have already discussed it. So whoever holds that it is possible to conceive His essence allows that He has a name corresponding to His specific reality, and whoever holds that it is impossible to conceive it does not allow a name taken from His essence, because the coining of a name for a meaning is a branch and a means to its understanding; if it cannot be conceived and understood, then a counterpart for it is inconceivable. But there is a discussion in this, for the disagreement about conceiving the essence of His essence and the coining of a name does not depend on it, since it is possible that one conceive an essence in some respect and the name is coined for its particularity and intended to make it understood by way of what is not its quiddity, and that respect may be a justifier for the coinage and external to the concept of the name, as was passed that the word 'Allah' is a proper name coined for His essence without any meaning considered in it. (And as for that taken from the part), like 'body' for a human for example, (it is impossible) for Him the Exalted, (because we have shown that essential necessity negates composition), so no part can be conceived for His essence such that a name is coined for it. (And as for that taken from the (external) attribute that enters into the concept of the name), (it is permissible) with respect to Him the Exalted. (Then this attribute may be real) like 'the All-Knowing', (or relational) like 'the Majestic' in the sense of the lofty, (or privative) like 'the Holy'. (And as for that taken from the action, it is permissible) with respect to Him the Exalted as well. (These mentioned divisions of the name are its simple divisions, and they may combine in pairs or more, and you will know their examples in what follows from this purpose).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a classification of names according to their derivation. The author explains that a name applied to a thing can be taken from four sources: the thing's essence, a part of it, an external attribute, or an action. He then applies this to Allah. Names taken from the essence depend on whether one can conceive His essence; those who affirm conceptual possibility allow such names (like 'Allah' itself, which is a proper name for His essence without connoting any meaning). Names taken from a part are impossible because Allah's essence is not composed. Names taken from an external attribute are permissible, and such attributes can be real (like 'al-ʿAlīm'), relational (like 'al-Mājid' meaning possessing lofty qualities), or privative (like 'al-Quddūs' meaning free from defects). Names taken from an action are also permissible. The author notes that these are the simple divisions; they can also be combined (e.g., compound names). The discussion sets the stage for the next section where examples will be given.

Key Terms
الذات
الجزء
الوصف الخارجي
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the fourfold classification by itself determines which names are valid for Allah.
Correction: The classification is general; the author explicitly says 'then we consider which of them is possible for Allah', meaning further reasoning is required.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the four ways a name can be taken?
A) Essence, part, attribute, action
B) Essence, accident, substance, action
C) Part, whole, attribute, relation
D) Essence, attribute, action, relation

Key Takeaway
The basic classification of names is by their derivation from essence, part, attribute, or action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by one of His names, do you stop to consider whether that name comes from His essence, attribute, or action? How might that awareness change the depth and sincerity of your supplication?
?
This classification shows that some categories—like 'part'—are impossible for Allah. Are there any names or concepts you have ever harbored in your heart about Allah that, upon reflection, should be impossible for Him? Will you actively uproot them?
?
If all knowledge of Allah comes through names derived from these sources, are you truly content to know Him only through what He has revealed? Or do you find yourself wishing for something beyond? What does that reveal about your trust in His revelation?

Names from Essence and Part: Possibility and Impossibility
al-Jurjani discusses the conditions for names taken from the essence and rejects names taken from a part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over names taken from Allah's essence and why names from a part are impossible.

Core Idea
Only the one who affirms conceiving Allah's essence can name Him from it; names from a part are impossible because Allah has no parts.

After the general classification, the author delves into two specific categories: names taken from the essence and those taken from a part. The former depends on whether one can conceive Allah's essence; the latter is categorically impossible due to Allah's simplicity. This passage also includes a nuanced discussion on what it means to name from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion on essence-names
Read the first part of the passage where the author presents two opposing views on naming from the essence.
Notice the argument that naming requires conception.
2
Understand the counterargument
The author introduces a subtle point: even if full knowledge of the quiddity is impossible, a name can be coined for a particularity conceived in some respect.
This is illustrated by the name 'Allah' itself.
3
Grasp the impossibility of part-names
Read the section on names taken from a part; the author gives a clear proof: essential necessity negates composition, so Allah cannot have parts.
This is a logical argument, not scriptural.

?

?

Permissible Divine Names: Attributes and Actions
al-Jurjani explains the permissibility and subtypes of attribute-based names and action-based names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three subtypes of attribute-based names and the permissibility of action-based names.

Core Idea
Names taken from attributes are permissible and can be real, relational, or privative; action-names are also permissible.

This lesson covers the remaining two categories: names taken from external attributes and those taken from actions. The author also subdivides attribute-based names into real, relational, and privative. Finally, he notes that these simple names can combine to form compound names, setting up the next section.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on attribute-names
Read the part that says names taken from external attributes are permissible, and note the three subtypes: real, relational, privative.
Examples are given for each.
2
Understand action-names
Read that names taken from actions are also permissible for Allah.
No examples are given here; they will follow.
3
Note the possibility of compounding
The author concludes that these are the simple divisions, and they can combine into compound names.
This anticipates the next section where examples are given.

Source Text

The Principle of Tawqīf in Divine Names
Al-Jurjani presents the dispute over naming Allah with attribute-derived names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between proper names and attribute-derived names, and the three theological positions on whether naming Allah with attribute-derived names requires scriptural permission.

Core Idea
Naming Allah with attribute-derived names requires divine permission according to the Ash'ari position.

In this passage, al-Jurjani introduces the third purpose of his discussion: the issue of tawqīf (divine permission) in naming Allah. He clarifies that the dispute is not about the proper names of Allah, but about names derived from attributes and actions. The passage then presents the views of the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya, who allow reasoning-based naming, and the Ash'ari view that requires scriptural permission, with a nuanced position from Qadi Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the central issue: whether naming Allah with attribute-derived names requires scriptural permission.
Pay attention to the distinction between proper names (like 'Allah') and derived names (like 'al-ʿAlīm').
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three views: the Mu'tazila/Karramiyya, Qadi Abu Bakr, and the Ash'ari (tawqīf). Find each one and note their key criteria.
The Ash'ari position is the main one: tawqīf.
3
Understand the clarification about proper names vs. derived names
The author explicitly states that the dispute is not about proper names like 'Allah' but about names taken from attributes and actions. This sets the scope of the discussion.
4
Consider why 'al-ʿārif' and 'al-faqīh' are prohibited
According to Qadi Abu Bakr, these terms are not permissible because they imply imperfection: 'al-ʿārif' suggests knowledge preceded by heedlessness, and 'al-faqīh' suggests understanding the speaker's intent, which is not fitting for Allah.
This illustrates the condition that names must not imply anything unbefitting.
5
Reflect on the overarching principle of tawqīf
The Ash'ari position holds that scriptural permission is required for naming Allah with attribute-derived names. This principle ensures that divine names are based on revelation, not human reason alone.

المقصد الثالث : تسميته تعالى بالاسماء توفيقية أي يتوقف إطلاقها على الإذن فيه ) وليس الكلام في أسمائه الأعلام الموضوعة في اللغات إنما النزاع في الأسماء المأخوذة من الصفات والأفعال، فذهبت المعتزلة والكرامية إلى أنه إذا دل العقل على اتصافه تعالى بصفة وجودية أو سلبية جاز أن يطلق عليه اسم يدل على اتصافه بها، سواء ورد بذلك الإطلاق إذن شرعي أو لم يرد وكذا الحال في الأفعال. وقال القاضي أبو بكر من أصحابنا، كل لفظ دل على معنى ثابت لله تعالى جاز إطلاقه عليه بلا توقيف إذا لم يكن إطلاقه موهماً لما لا يليق بكبريائه، فمن ثمة لم يجز أن يطلق عليه لفظ العارف لأن المعرفة قد يراد بها علم يسبقه غفلة ولا لفظ الفقيه لأن الفقه فهم غرض المتكلم مذهب الأشعرية، لأنه إنما يفيد تعقل الواضع للمسمى، ولا يفيد إمكان تفهيمه المعتبر أيضاً في وضع الاسم . قوله : ( أي يتوقف إطلاقها على الإذن فيه فإن قلت من الأوصاف ما يمتنع إطلاقه عليه تعالى مع ورود الشرع بها كالماكر والمستهزئ ونظائرهما . قلت : أجاب في شرح المقاصد بأنه لا يكفي في الإذن مجرد وقوعها في الكتاب والسنة بحسب اقتضاء المقام وسياق الكلام، بل يجب أن لا يخلو عن نوع تعظيم ورعاية أدب . قوله : ( وقال القاضي أبو بكر إلخ حجته قياس لوازم الأسماء على مرادفاتها، واعترض عليه إمام الحرمين بأن القياس إنما يعتبر في العمليات دون الأسماء والصفات، وأجيب : بأن التسمية عمل اللسان .
The third purpose: naming Him the Exalted with names is by divine permission (tawfīqiyya), i.e., their application depends on permission therein. The discussion is not about His proper names placed in languages; rather the dispute is about names taken from attributes and actions. The Mu'tazila and Karramiyya held that if reason indicates that He the Exalted is described by an existential or privative attribute, it is permissible to apply to Him a name indicating His being described by it, whether scriptural permission for that application has come or not, and similarly for actions. And Qadi Abu Bakr from our companions held: every word that indicates a meaning established for Allah the Exalted is permissible to apply to Him without tawqīf if its application does not suggest what is unbefitting of His majesty. Hence it is not permissible to apply to Him the term 'al-ʿārif' (the knower) because knowledge (al-maʿrifa) might imply knowledge preceded by heedlessness, nor the term 'al-faqīh' because fiqh is understanding the speaker's intent. (This is) the doctrine of the Ashʿariyya, because it only conveys the conceptualization (taʿaqqul) of the coiner for the named, but does not convey the possibility of its understanding (tafhīm) that is also considered in the coining of a name. His saying: (i.e., their application depends on permission therein. For if you say: Among the descriptions there are those whose application to Him the Exalted is prohibited despite the occurrence of the law with them, like al-mākir (the deceiver) and al-mustahziʾ (the mocker) and their likes. I say: He answered in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that mere occurrence in the Book and the Sunna according to the requirement of the context and the flow of speech is not sufficient for permission; rather it must not be devoid of a kind of glorification and observance of propriety.) His saying: (And Qadi Abu Bakr said etc. His proof is analogizing the concomitants of names to their synonyms. Imām al-Ḥaramayn objected to him that analogy is only considered in practical matters, not in names and attributes. And it was answered: because naming is an act of the tongue.)
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage discusses whether naming Allah with names derived from attributes or actions requires scriptural permission (tawqīf). The author begins by clarifying that the dispute is not about proper names like 'Allah', but about names indicating attributes or actions. The Mu'tazila and Karramiyya hold that if reason affirms that Allah has a certain attribute, the corresponding name may be used even without scriptural warrant. Qadi Abu Bakr (an Ashʿarī) holds a middle position: any word that denotes a meaning truly established for Allah is permissible as long as it does not imply something unworthy of His majesty; thus 'knower' (ʿārif) and 'jurist' (faqīh) are prohibited because they carry connotations of deficiency (heedlessness or dependence). The author then reports an objection from Imām al-Ḥaramayn that analogy (qiyās) is valid in practical law but not in matters of divine names and attributes; the defense is that naming is a verbal act and thus falls under practical law. The passage also addresses a potential objection: some terms like 'deceiver' and 'mocker' appear in the Quran but are not used as names for Allah because they lack dignity and respect; mere occurrence in scripture is insufficient without consideration of context and propriety.

Key Terms
توقيف
تسمية
اسم
صفة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if reason affirms an attribute for Allah, the corresponding name can be used without scriptural warrant.
Correction: The Ashʿari position requires scriptural permission (tawqīf) for applying attribute-derived names to Allah, even if reason affirms the attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Qadi Abu Bakr, what condition must be met for a name to be permissible for Allah?
A) It must appear in the Quran or Sunna.
B) It must not imply anything unbefitting His majesty.
C) It must be a proper name.
D) It must be derived from an action.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on divine names requires scriptural permission (tawqīf), unlike the Mu'tazila who rely solely on reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah in your personal supplications or thoughts, do you ever use names or attributes that you have not learned from the Quran or Sunnah? What would justify using such a name before the One who has revealed His own names?
?
The Mu'tazila believed reason alone could permit naming Allah, but the Ash'aris insist on tawqīf. In your own faith, where do you find yourself trusting your intellect over the text? How does that affect your reverence for Allah's perfection?
?
The name 'al-ʿārif' is prohibited because it implies knowledge preceded by ignorance. How does the eternal perfection of Allah's knowledge challenge the pride you take in your own learning and understanding?

Objections and the Qiyās Argument in Naming Allah
Al-Jurjani addresses objections and defends the use of analogy in naming Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection about Quranic terms like 'deceiver' and the debate over using analogy (qiyās) to justify divine names.

Core Idea
Mere occurrence in revelation is not enough; names must convey dignity, and analogy in naming Allah is defended as a verbal act.

The passage continues by addressing a potential objection: if naming requires permission, how can terms like 'deceiver' and 'mocker' appear in the Quran but not be used as names? The answer emphasizes context and propriety. Then the discussion shifts to Qadi Abu Bakr's proof using analogy, Imam al-Haramayn's objection that qiyās applies only to practical matters, and the response that naming is an act of the tongue.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its answer
Read the part about the objection concerning Quranic terms like 'deceiver' and 'mocker'. Notice that the answer explains that mere occurrence is not enough; the context and dignity must be considered.
This shows that tawqīf is not a simple checklist but requires consideration of propriety.
2
Understand the qiyās debate
Read the exchange between Qadi Abu Bakr and Imam al-Haramayn. Qadi Abu Bakr uses analogy to justify names; Imam al-Haramayn objects that analogy is only for practical matters; the defense is that naming is a verbal act.
This is a key methodological point about how theology and jurisprudence interact.
3
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how these two parts (objection and qiyās debate) reinforce the Ashʿari principle of tawqīf and show its complexity.

Classification of Divine Names from Essence, Part, Attribute, and Action
Classification of divine names into essence, part, attribute, and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of divine names based on their derivation and why names taken from a part are impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Divine names are classified by derivation; part-derived names are impossible due to essential necessity.

The classification of divine names is a foundational topic in kalam, clarifying which names can be applied to Allah and why. This passage from Sanusi systematically lays out the categories, beginning with the name 'Allah' as a proper noun and then discussing names derived from parts, attributes, and actions. Understanding this classification prevents conceptual errors about Allah's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a reminder about 'Allah', then four categories with rulings.
The passage uses a clear enumeration pattern.
2
Identify the four categories
Find the four divisions: 1) taken from essence (Allah), 2) taken from a part (impossible), 3) taken from an attribute (permissible, with three subtypes), 4) taken from an action (permissible).
Note that the first category is just mentioned as a proper noun, not in the same list.
3
Understand the reasoning for impossibility
Focus on why names taken from a part are impossible: because essential necessity precludes composition. Ask yourself what 'part' means here.
Part implies composition, which contradicts wujub dhati.
4
Distinguish the subtypes of attribute-derived names
The passage mentions real (haqiqi), relational (idafi), and privative (salbi) attributes. Think of examples: 'Alim, Majid, Quddus.
Real attributes denote a positive perfection; relational denote relationship; privative negate imperfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على ما مر من أن لفظ الله اسم علم له موضوع لذاته من غير اعتبار معنى فيه ( وأما المأخوذ من الجزء) كالجسم للإنسان مثلاً ( فمحال عليه ) تعالى ( لما بينا من أن الوجوب الذاتي ينافي التركيب ( فلا يتصور لذاته تعالى جزء حتى يطلق اسمه عليه ( وأما المأخوذ من الوصف (الخارجي) الداخل في مفهوم الاسم ( فجائز) في حقه تعالى ( ثم هذا الوصف قد يكون حقيقياً ) كالعليم ( وقد يكون إضافياً) كالماجد بمعنى المعالي ويكون سلبياً) كالقدوس، ( وأما المأخوذ من الفعل فجائز) في حقه تعالى أيضاً (فهذه الأقسام المذكورة للاسم هي ( أقسامه البسيطة وقد تتركب ثنائياً وأكثر، وستعلم أمثلتها فيما يتبعه من المقصد ) .
As has passed that the word Allah is a proper noun (ism 'alam) placed for His essence without consideration of any meaning in it. And as for (that taken from a part), like 'body' for human for example, (it is impossible) for Him, exalted, (because we have shown that essential necessity (wujub dhati) precludes composition (tarkib)), so a part is not conceivable for His essence such that His name would be given from it. And as for (that taken from a descriptive attribute (wasf)) (external) that is included in the concept of the name, (it is permissible) with respect to Him, exalted. Then this attribute may be real (haqiqi) like al-'Alim, or relational (idafi) like al-Majid meaning the lofty, or privative (salbi) like al-Quddus. And as for (that taken from an action (fi'l)), it is also permissible with respect to Him, exalted. So these mentioned divisions of the name are its simple divisions, and they may be combined (murakkab) in twos or more, and you will learn their examples in what follows of this section.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage classifies the names of Allah according to their derivation. It begins by recalling that the name 'Allah' is a proper noun placed for the essence without meaning. Then it enumerates four categories: (1) names taken from a part—impossible because Allah's essential necessity precludes composition; (2) names taken from an external attribute that enters the concept—permissible, and this attribute can be real (like 'Alim), relational (like Majid), or privative (like Quddus); (3) names taken from an action—also permissible. These are the simple divisions, and they can be combined. The section promises examples later.

Key Terms
اسم علم
وجوب ذاتي
تركيب
وصف حقيقي
وصف إضافي
وصف سلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that names derived from attributes imply composition in Allah.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between part and attribute; attributes are not parts. Names from attributes are permissible and do not imply composition.

Checkpoint
Which category of names is impossible for Allah according to this classification?
A) Names taken from an attribute
B) Names taken from an action
C) Names taken from a part
D) Names taken from an essence

Key Takeaway
Divine names derived from parts are impossible for Allah, while those from attributes (real, relational, privative) and actions are permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you unconsciously reduce Him to something composite or limited by a part? How will you ensure your understanding of His names remains free from any notion of composition?
?
The name 'Allah' is a proper noun without meaning. Yet you often treat it as if it is just another attribute. How does forgetting that 'Allah' is His unique name affect the intimacy of your du'a?
?
If attributes like al-Quddus (privative) negate imperfection, then what imperfections in your own character are you silently defending? Are you willing to let Allah's perfection expose your flaws?

The Tawfiqiyyah of Divine Names: Three Positions
States the three positions on tawfiqiyyah of divine names and the related debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on tawfiqiyyah of divine names: the rationalist view, the permissibility view, and the strict tawfiq view, along with the key objections and responses.

Core Idea
Divine names require either scriptural permission or careful rational justification to avoid disrespect.

This passage from Sanusi presents the third section on divine names, addressing whether naming Allah is tawfiqiyyah—requiring scriptural authorization. It clarifies that the debate concerns names derived from attributes and acts, not proper names. You will encounter three key positions and a discussion on how revelation relates to naming.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scope of the debate
Read the opening sentence: naming Allah is tawfiqiyyah, and the discussion is about names taken from attributes and acts, not proper names. This sets the boundaries.
Tawfiqiyyah means requiring divine permission.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila/Karramiyya position
They hold that if reason indicates an attribute, it is permissible to name Allah with it, regardless of scriptural permission. This is the most permissive view.
This includes both positive and privative attributes.
3
Learn Qadi Abu Bakr's position
He allows any word indicating a true meaning of Allah as long as it does not imply anything unbefitting. He gives examples of prohibited names like 'arif and faqih.
This is a middle ground, requiring caution.
4
Address the objection about scriptural attributes
An objector points out that some attributes appear in revelation (e.g., al-makir) yet are not used as names. The answer explains that context and respect are necessary.
Mere occurrence does not suffice; respect is required.
5
Analyze the qiyas debate
Qadi Abu Bakr's argument uses analogy (qiyas) of implications. Imam al-Haramayn objects that qiyas applies only to practical matters, but the response says naming is a practice of the tongue.
This highlights a methodological disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : تسميته تعالى بالاسماء توفيقية أي يتوقف إطلاقها على الإذن فيه ) وليس الكلام في أسمائه الأعلام الموضوعة في اللغات إنما النزاع في الأسماء المأخوذة من الصفات والأفعال، فذهبت المعتزلة والكرامية إلى أنه إذا دل العقل على اتصافه تعالى بصفة وجودية أو سلبية جاز أن يطلق عليه اسم يدل على اتصافه بها، سواء ورد بذلك الإطلاق إذن شرعي أو لم يرد وكذا الحال في الأفعال. وقال القاضي أبو بكر من أصحابنا، كل لفظ دل على معنى ثابت لله تعالى جاز إطلاقه عليه بلا توقيف إذا لم يكن إطلاقه موهماً لما لا يليق بكبريائه، فمن ثمة لم يجز أن يطلق عليه لفظ العارف لأن المعرفة قد يراد بها علم يسبقه غفلة ولا لفظ الفقيه لأن الفقه فهم غرض المتكلم مذهب الأشعرية، لأنه إنما يفيد تعقل الواضع للمسمى، ولا يفيد إمكان تفهيمه المعتبر أيضاً في وضع الاسم . قوله : ( أي يتوقف إطلاقها على الإذن فيه فإن قلت من الأوصاف ما يمتنع إطلاقه عليه تعالى مع ورود الشرع بها كالماكر والمستهزئ ونظائرهما . قلت : أجاب في شرح المقاصد بأنه لا يكفي في الإذن مجرد وقوعها في الكتاب والسنة بحسب اقتضاء المقام وسياق الكلام، بل يجب أن لا يخلو عن نوع تعظيم ورعاية أدب . قوله : ( وقال القاضي أبو بكر إلخ حجته قياس لوازم الأسماء على مرادفاتها، واعترض عليه إمام الحرمين بأن القياس إنما يعتبر في العمليات دون الأسماء والصفات، وأجيب : بأن التسمية عمل اللسان .
The third section: naming Allah with names is tawfiqiyyah, i.e., their application depends on permission for it. And the discussion is not about His proper names in languages; rather the dispute is about names taken from attributes and acts. The Mu'tazila and Karramiyya held that if the intellect indicates that He, exalted, is described by a positive or privative attribute, it is permissible to apply to Him a name indicating His being described by it, whether scriptural permission for that application has come or not, and similarly for acts. And al-Qadi Abu Bakr from our companions said: Every word that indicates a meaning established for Allah, exalted, is permissible to apply to Him without tawfiq as long as its application does not suggest something unbefitting His majesty. Hence it is not permissible to apply to Him the word 'arif because knowledge (ma'rifa) may mean knowledge preceded by heedlessness, nor the word faqih because fiqh is understanding the intent of the speaker—the school of the Ash'ariyya, because it only conveys the conceptualization of the namer of the named, and does not convey the possibility of its communication also considered in placing the name. He said: (i.e., its application depends on permission for it) If you say: Among attributes, there are some whose application to Him is impossible even though scripture mentions them, like al-makir (the deceiver) and al-mustahzi' (the mocker) and their likes. I say: It was answered in Sharh al-Maqasid that mere occurrence in the Book and Sunnah according to the requirement of the context and discourse is not sufficient for permission; rather it must not be devoid of some exaltation and observance of etiquette. He said: (And al-Qadi Abu Bakr said, etc.) His argument is the analogy (qiyas) of the implications of names with their synonyms. And Imam al-Haramayn objected that analogy is only considered in practical matters (al-`amaliyyat), not in names and attributes. And it was answered: that naming is an action of the tongue.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the central debate on whether divine names require scriptural authorization (tawfiq) or can be derived rationally. It specifies that the dispute concerns names taken from attributes and acts, not proper names. Three positions are stated: (1) Mu'tazila and Karramiyya allow rational derivation without scriptural evidence; (2) Qadi Abu Bakr allows it as long as the name does not imply what is unbefitting, giving examples like 'arif and faqih; (3) the Ash'ari position (implied) requires tawfiq. An objection is raised: some attributes like 'deceiver' appear in revelation but cannot be used as names. The answer clarifies that such occurrences are contextual and lack respectful tone. Finally, Qadi Abu Bakr's argument is based on analogy of implications, but Imam al-Haramayn objects that analogy applies only to practical rulings; the response is that naming is a practice of the tongue.

Key Terms
توفيقية
اسم علم
معتزلة
كرامية
قاضي أبو بكر
قياس
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any name that appears in the Quran or Hadith can be used as a divine name without restriction.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that mere occurrence is not sufficient; the name must also convey exaltation and respect. Attributes like 'deceiver' are not used despite their appearance.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya, what is the criterion for naming Allah with a name derived from an attribute?
A) Only names from revelation
B) Rational indication of the attribute suffices
C) Only names that are not misleading
D) Names must be from both reason and revelation

Key Takeaway
Divine naming requires tawfiq (permission) or at least careful rational justification to ensure no disrespect, as shown by the three positions and the objection about scriptural attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you consider whether you are using them with the proper reverence and exaltation, or do you sometimes treat them as mere labels? How can you ensure your tongue respects His greatness?
?
The source warns against using names that might imply deficiency, even if they occur in revelation. Reflect: Are there any words or phrases you casually use about Allah that, upon reflection, might lack the required adab? Will you investigate and replace them?
?
The debate here shows the scholars' extreme care in naming Allah. Does your own heart and tongue reflect that same caution, or have you been negligent in presuming that any 'good' word is fine? What step will you take today to increase your reverence in dhikr?
