Impermissible Names for Allah: Examples and Reasoning
Enumeration of disallowed names with their linguistic explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain names like 'arif, faqih, 'aqil, fatin, and tabib are impermissible for Allah due to their connotations of human imperfection.

Core Idea
Names that imply prior ignorance or constraint cannot befit Allah.

This passage examines specific names that may seem positive but contain hidden implications of imperfection. Even if reason suggests they could be used respectfully, their linguistic roots reveal deficiencies incompatible with divine perfection. Let's explore each example.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of names
Read the Arabic names: 'arif, faqih, 'aqil, fatin, tabib. Note their literal meanings as given.
Each name is explained with its root meaning.
2
Identify the common flaw
For each name, notice the flaw: prior heedlessness, ignorance, constraint, quickness derived from ignorance, or experience-based knowledge.
3
Reflect on rational permissibility
Consider that despite these names being positive in human context, they are disallowed for Allah due to subtle imperfections.
This shows the high standard required for divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن ثمة لم يجز أن يطلق عليه لفظ العارف لأن المعرفة قد يراد بها علم يسبقه غفلة ولا لفظ الفقيه لأن الفقه فهم غرض المتكلم كلامه وذلك مشعر بسابقة الجهل ولا لفظ العاقل لأن العقل علم مانع عن الإقدام على ما لا ينبغي مأخوذ من العقال، وإنما يتصور هذا المعنى فيمن يدعوه الداعي إلى ما لا ينبغي، ولا لفظ الفطن لأن الفطانة سرعة إدراك ما يراد تعريضه على السامع فتكون مسبوقة بالجهل، ولا لفظ الطبيب لأن الطب يراد به علم مأخوذ من التجارب إلى غير ذلك من الأسماء التي فيها نوع إيهام بما لا يصح في حقه تعالى. وقد يقال : بد مع نفي ذلك الإيهام من الإشعار بالتعظيم حتى يصح الإطلاق بلا توقيف، وذهب الشيخ ومتابعوه إلى أنه لا بد من التوقيف وهو المختار ( وذلك للاحتياط احترازاً عما يوهم باطلاً لعظم الخطر في ذلك فلا يجوز الاكتفاء في عدم إيهام الباطل بمبلغ إدراكنا بل لا بد من الاستناد إلى إذن الشرع )
Hence it is not permissible to apply to Him the word 'arif because knowledge (ma'rifa) may mean knowledge preceded by heedlessness, nor the word faqih because fiqh is understanding the intent of the speaker—his speech suggesting prior ignorance—nor the word 'aqil because 'aql is knowledge that prevents one from rushing into what is not proper, derived from 'iqal (a hobble), and this meaning is only conceivable in one whom the impulse calls to what is not proper, nor the word fatin because fatanah is quick apprehension of what is intended to be alluded to the listener, thus being preceded by ignorance, nor the word tabib because medicine (tibb) is meant as knowledge derived from experiences, and so on of the names that contain a type of illusion of what is not correct with respect to Him, exalted. And it may be said: Having negated that illusion by indicating exaltation, application without tawfiq would be valid. But the Sheikh and his followers held that tawfiq is necessary, and it is the chosen view—(this is for caution, guarding against what implies falsehood due to the great danger in that; so it is not permissible to rely on our own perception in negating the illusion of falsehood; rather dependence on scriptural permission is necessary.)
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage provides specific examples of names that are not permissible even though reason might suggest them. The names 'arif (knower), faqih (jurist), 'aqil (intelligent), fatin (quick-witted), and tabib (physician) are all disallowed because their meanings include connotations of human imperfections like preceding ignorance or external constraints. It then presents a possible objection: if the illusion of imperfection is removed by adding an indication of exaltation, could the name be used without tawfiq? The response, which is the chosen view of the Sheikh (Ash'ari), insists that caution requires scriptural permission, as our own judgment cannot guarantee the absence of misleading implications.

Key Terms
عارف
فقيه
عاقل
فطن
طبيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It implies knowledge from experience, which suggests imperfection
B) It is not mentioned in the Quran
C) It is too worldly
D) It is a synonym for a prohibited name

Key Takeaway
Even honorable human titles can carry subtle imperfections that make them unfit for Allah; careful linguistic analysis is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these names (arif, faqih, aqil, fatin, tabib) do you most admire in people you know? How does realizing that even these noble human qualities are tainted with imperfection change your awe of Allah's complete perfection?
?
You may have used the word 'Knower' for Allah without thinking deeply. After this lesson, you understand that even the name 'arif is not allowed. Ask yourself: Have I ever carelessly described Allah with a term that carries hidden flaws? What will you do from now on to ensure your words about Allah reflect absolute perfection?
?
Allah is called al-‘Alim (the All-Knowing) and al-Hakim (the All-Wise). But this lesson warns against using names that imply learning or experience. When you speak of Allah's knowledge, do you truly believe it is without beginning, without learning, and without limitation? If so, why do you sometimes think of Him as 'understanding' you like a human would? Write one concrete change in how you pray or praise Allah starting today.

The Principle of Caution: Tawfiq is Required
Objection and response asserting necessity of scriptural permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that even if an illusion of imperfection is removed, scriptural permission is still needed for naming Allah.

Core Idea
Caution requires going beyond human reasoning and seeking divine authorization.

After listing impermissible names, a potential objection arises: if we add a safeguard to negate the illusion, could the name then be used? The Ash'ari response emphasizes caution and reliance on divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: 'if we negate the illusion by indicating exaltation, then application without tawfiq would be valid.'
This is a rational counterargument.
2
Understand the response
The Sheikh and his followers reject this objection. They insist on tawfiq (scriptural permission) as necessary.
The reason given is caution against any falsehood.
3
Appreciate the principle
Human reasoning cannot fully guarantee that a name is free from false implications. Only divine authorization provides certainty.
This reflects the Ash'ari methodological principle of tawfiq.

The Ninety-Nine Names and Their Specification
Enumerates the hadith and clarifies specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith of the ninety-nine names, its source in the Sahihs, and that the exact list was specified by later scholars like al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi, while acknowledging that tawfiq has come for other names as well.

Core Idea
The ninety-nine names are established by tawfiq, but their exact list comes from later scholars, not the hadith itself.

This lesson opens the discussion of the divine names by focusing on the well-known hadith from the two Sahihs about Allah having ninety-nine names. The commentator notes that while the hadith does not enumerate them, later traditionists like al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi compiled the list. The phrase 'in the well-known' indicates that other names are also permitted by tawfiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the key terms: 'التوقيف', 'المشهور', 'الصحيحين'. Then read the translation to understand the flow.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مائة إلا واحداً'.
2
Identify the two key points
Notice that the hadith does not specify the names, but later scholars did. Also note the significance of the phrase 'in the well-known'.
3
Reflect on the concept of tawfiq
Tawfiq means that the names are based on divine revelation. This passage establishes that the ninety-nine are the most famous, but others exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي ورد به التوقيف في المشهور تسعة اسماء تسعة وتسعون ، فقد ورد في الصحيحين أن لله تسعة وتسعين اسماً، مائة إلا واحداً من أحصاها دخل الجنة . ليس فيهما تعيين تلك الأسماء لكن الترمذي والبيهقي عيناها كما في الكتاب وإنما قال : في ( المشهور إذ قد ورد التوقيف بغيرها قوله : ( ولا لفظ الطبيب ) اعترض عليه بأنه قد ورد في حديث المصابيح أنه عليه السلام قال لمن قال : إني طبيب : أنت رفيق والله الطبيب، وقد عرفت مما نقلناه عن شرح المقاصد جوابه . قوله : ( وذهب الشيخ ومتابعوه إلخ هاهنا فائدة ينبغي أن ينبه عليها وهو أن بعض الفضلاء صرح بأن لفظ واجب الوجود لم يرد في الكتاب والسنة فمن اشترط التوقيف في أسماء الله تعالى ينبغي أن لا يجوز إطلاقه عليه تعالى مع أن هذا الإطلاق شائع، اللهم إلا أن يقال : هذا في التحقيق صفة جرت على غير من هي له إذ المعنى واجب وجوده كما في حسن الوجه فليس من محل النزاع فليتأمل. قوله : ( مئة إلا واحداً تأكيد لقوله تسعة وتسعين قيل : وفائدته دفع توهم أن العبارة سبعة وتسعين أو سبعين وفي بعض الروايات إلا واحدة فتأنثيت واحدة على تأويل الاسم بالكلمة . قوله : (إذ قد ورد التوقيف بغيرها إلخ) فإن قلت: إذا كانت أسماء الله تعالى زائدة على التسع والتسعين فما معنى الحصر المستفاد من الحديث المنقول من صحيح البخاري ومسلم ؟ قلت : قوله عليه السلام : من أحصاها دخل الجنة في موقع الصفة لقوله: تسعة وتسعين اسماً ثم إن أسماء الله تعالى يجوز أن تتفاوت فضيلتها لتفاوت معانيها في الجلالة والشرف فيكون تسعة وتسعون اسما منها بأعيانها يجمع أنواعاً من المعاني المنبئة عن الحلال لا يجمع ذلك غيرها فإن قلت : اسم الله تعالى الأعظم لم يدخل في التسعة والتسعين، فكيف يختص بمزيد الشرف ما هو خارج عنها وإن دخل فكيف ذلك وهي مشهورة والاسم الأعظم يختص بمعرفته ولي أو نبي وهي سبب كرامات عظيمة لمن عرفه قلت يحتمل الخروج ويكون شرف هذه الأسماء المعدودة الأسماء المشهورة عند الجماهير لا بالإضافة إلى الأسماء التي يختص
And what is established by tawfiq in the well-known is ninety-nine names. It is reported in the two Sahihs that Allah has ninety-nine names, one hundred minus one; whoever enumerates them enters paradise. These two do not specify those names, but al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi specified them as in the book. And he only said: 'in the well-known' because tawfiq has come for others. He said: (And the word 'tabib') objection was raised that it appears in the hadith of the lamps that he, peace be upon him, said to the one who said 'I am a physician': 'You are a companion and Allah is the physician.' And you have known from what we transmitted from Sharh al-Maqasid its answer. He said: (And the Sheikh and his followers, etc.) Here is a benefit that should be noted: some virtuous persons stated that the word 'Wajib al-Wujud' does not occur in the Book and Sunnah, so whoever stipulates tawfiq in the names of Allah should not permit its application to Him, although this application is widespread. Unless it is said: This is in reality an attribute applied to other than its proper subject, for the meaning is 'necessary of existence' as in 'beautiful of face', so it is not from the subject of dispute. Let it be pondered. He said: (One hundred minus one is an emphasis for ninety-nine; it is said its benefit is to remove the misconception that the expression is ninety-seven or seventy, and in some narrations 'except one' (illa wahidah), so 'wahidah' is feminine because the name is interpreted as a word. He said: (Since tawfiq has come for others) If you say: If the names of Allah exceed ninety-nine, what is the meaning of the limitation derived from the hadith narrated from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim? I say: His words 'whoever enumerates them enters paradise' is in the position of an attribute for 'ninety-nine names'. Then the names of Allah may differ in virtue due to the difference of their meanings in majesty and honor, so ninety-nine specific names combine types of meanings that indicate what is lawful, which others do not combine. If you say: The greatest name of Allah is not included in the ninety-nine, so how can what is outside them be specially honored? And if it is included, how can that be, since they are well-known and the greatest name is known only to a saint or prophet, and it is the cause of great miracles for those who know it? I say: It is possible that it is outside, and the honor of these counted names is the well-known names among the masses, not in addition to the names that are specific...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage discusses several connected issues. First, it states that the well-known names permitted by tawfiq are the ninety-nine mentioned in hadith from the two Sahihs, though the hadith does not specify them; later scholars like al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi did. Then an objection is raised: the word 'tabib' appears in a hadith (the Prophet said 'Allah is the physician'), so why is it disallowed? The answer is assumed known from Sharh al-Maqasid. Next, a benefit is noted: the term 'Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existence) is not in the Quran or Sunnah, so strict tawfiq would seem to forbid it. The response is that it is actually a descriptive phrase predicated of something else (like 'beautiful of face'), not a proper name, so it falls outside the dispute. Then there is a discussion of the phrase 'one hundred minus one'—it is an emphasis and prevents misunderstanding the count. Finally, it addresses a question: if there are more than ninety-nine names, what does the hadith's limitation mean? The answer: the ninety-nine are those that combine all praiseworthy meanings; others are not excluded. It then raises the issue of the greatest name: if it is not among the ninety-nine, how can it be greater? The response is that it may be outside, and the honor of the ninety-nine is relative to common knowledge, not to the exclusive names.

Key Terms
تسعة وتسعون
التوقيف
المشهور
الصحيحين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the hadith itself lists the ninety-nine names.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the list was compiled by later scholars like al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi, not given in the hadith.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the specification of the ninety-nine names?
A) They are specified in the Sahihs
B) They were specified by al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi
C) They are not specified at all
D) They are specified in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The hadith of ninety-nine names is authentic but its specific list is from later traditionists, not from the Prophet's words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have trusted the list of ninety-nine names as coming directly from the Prophet, but now you learn the list was compiled by scholars. Does this reduce your certainty in the list? How will you approach using these names in your worship from now on?
?
The hadith promises paradise for those who enumerate the names. If the list is not explicit in the hadith, how have you been ensuring you are enumerating the correct list? Will you verify your practice against scholars' compilations?
?
The author mentions that tawfiq has come for other names beyond the ninety-nine. Do you restrict yourself to only these ninety-nine in your dhikr? What does this teach you about the vastness of Allah's attributes and your own need to expand your knowledge and remembrance of Him?

The Objection of the Word 'Tabib'
Presents objection and references answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised about the word 'tabib' (physician) and how it is answered by referring to a hadith and the commentary of Sharh al-Maqasid.

Core Idea
The word 'tabib' is used for Allah in a hadith, so why is it not allowed? The answer lies in the nuanced distinction between divine names and attributes.

In the previous section, it was stated that only certain names are permitted by tawfiq. An objection is raised: the word 'tabib' (physician) appears in a hadith where the Prophet says 'Allah is the physician.' How then can it be disallowed? This lesson presents the objection and directs the reader to the answer found in Sharh al-Maqasid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage where the objector cites the hadith of the lamps.
Note the key phrase 'حديث المصابيح'.
2
Understand the hadith cited
The hadith shows the Prophet saying 'Allah is the physician' to someone who called himself a physician.
3
Recognize the answer is referenced
The author does not give the full answer here but refers to Sharh al-Maqasid, indicating that the issue is about the precise meaning of 'tabib' as a name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي ورد به التوقيف في المشهور تسعة اسماء تسعة وتسعون ، فقد ورد في الصحيحين أن لله تسعة وتسعين اسماً، مائة إلا واحداً من أحصاها دخل الجنة . ليس فيهما تعيين تلك الأسماء لكن الترمذي والبيهقي عيناها كما في الكتاب وإنما قال : في ( المشهور إذ قد ورد التوقيف بغيرها قوله : ( ولا لفظ الطبيب ) اعترض عليه بأنه قد ورد في حديث المصابيح أنه عليه السلام قال لمن قال : إني طبيب : أنت رفيق والله الطبيب، وقد عرفت مما نقلناه عن شرح المقاصد جوابه . قوله : ( وذهب الشيخ ومتابعوه إلخ هاهنا فائدة ينبغي أن ينبه عليها وهو أن بعض الفضلاء صرح بأن لفظ واجب الوجود لم يرد في الكتاب والسنة فمن اشترط التوقيف في أسماء الله تعالى ينبغي أن لا يجوز إطلاقه عليه تعالى مع أن هذا الإطلاق شائع، اللهم إلا أن يقال : هذا في التحقيق صفة جرت على غير من هي له إذ المعنى واجب وجوده كما في حسن الوجه فليس من محل النزاع فليتأمل. قوله : ( مئة إلا واحداً تأكيد لقوله تسعة وتسعين قيل : وفائدته دفع توهم أن العبارة سبعة وتسعين أو سبعين وفي بعض الروايات إلا واحدة فتأنثيت واحدة على تأويل الاسم بالكلمة . قوله : (إذ قد ورد التوقيف بغيرها إلخ) فإن قلت: إذا كانت أسماء الله تعالى زائدة على التسع والتسعين فما معنى الحصر المستفاد من الحديث المنقول من صحيح البخاري ومسلم ؟ قلت : قوله عليه السلام : من أحصاها دخل الجنة في موقع الصفة لقوله: تسعة وتسعين اسماً ثم إن أسماء الله تعالى يجوز أن تتفاوت فضيلتها لتفاوت معانيها في الجلالة والشرف فيكون تسعة وتسعون اسما منها بأعيانها يجمع أنواعاً من المعاني المنبئة عن الحلال لا يجمع ذلك غيرها فإن قلت : اسم الله تعالى الأعظم لم يدخل في التسعة والتسعين، فكيف يختص بمزيد الشرف ما هو خارج عنها وإن دخل فكيف ذلك وهي مشهورة والاسم الأعظم يختص بمعرفته ولي أو نبي وهي سبب كرامات عظيمة لمن عرفه قلت يحتمل الخروج ويكون شرف هذه الأسماء المعدودة الأسماء المشهورة عند الجماهير لا بالإضافة إلى الأسماء التي يختص
And what is established by tawfiq in the well-known is ninety-nine names. It is reported in the two Sahihs that Allah has ninety-nine names, one hundred minus one; whoever enumerates them enters paradise. These two do not specify those names, but al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi specified them as in the book. And he only said: 'in the well-known' because tawfiq has come for others. He said: (And the word 'tabib') objection was raised that it appears in the hadith of the lamps that he, peace be upon him, said to the one who said 'I am a physician': 'You are a companion and Allah is the physician.' And you have known from what we transmitted from Sharh al-Maqasid its answer. He said: (And the Sheikh and his followers, etc.) Here is a benefit that should be noted: some virtuous persons stated that the word 'Wajib al-Wujud' does not occur in the Book and Sunnah, so whoever stipulates tawfiq in the names of Allah should not permit its application to Him, although this application is widespread. Unless it is said: This is in reality an attribute applied to other than its proper subject, for the meaning is 'necessary of existence' as in 'beautiful of face', so it is not from the subject of dispute. Let it be pondered. He said: (One hundred minus one is an emphasis for ninety-nine; it is said its benefit is to remove the misconception that the expression is ninety-seven or seventy, and in some narrations 'except one' (illa wahidah), so 'wahidah' is feminine because the name is interpreted as a word. He said: (Since tawfiq has come for others) If you say: If the names of Allah exceed ninety-nine, what is the meaning of the limitation derived from the hadith narrated from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim? I say: His words 'whoever enumerates them enters paradise' is in the position of an attribute for 'ninety-nine names'. Then the names of Allah may differ in virtue due to the difference of their meanings in majesty and honor, so ninety-nine specific names combine types of meanings that indicate what is lawful, which others do not combine. If you say: The greatest name of Allah is not included in the ninety-nine, so how can what is outside them be specially honored? And if it is included, how can that be, since they are well-known and the greatest name is known only to a saint or prophet, and it is the cause of great miracles for those who know it? I say: It is possible that it is outside, and the honor of these counted names is the well-known names among the masses, not in addition to the names that are specific...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage discusses several connected issues. First, it states that the well-known names permitted by tawfiq are the ninety-nine mentioned in hadith from the two Sahihs, though the hadith does not specify them; later scholars like al-Tirmidhi and al-Bayhaqi did. Then an objection is raised: the word 'tabib' appears in a hadith (the Prophet said 'Allah is the physician'), so why is it disallowed? The answer is assumed known from Sharh al-Maqasid. Next, a benefit is noted: the term 'Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existence) is not in the Quran or Sunnah, so strict tawfiq would seem to forbid it. The response is that it is actually a descriptive phrase predicated of something else (like 'beautiful of face'), not a proper name, so it falls outside the dispute. Then there is a discussion of the phrase 'one hundred minus one'—it is an emphasis and prevents misunderstanding the count. Finally, it addresses a question: if there are more than ninety-nine names, what does the hadith's limitation mean? The answer: the ninety-nine are those that combine all praiseworthy meanings; others are not excluded. It then raises the issue of the greatest name: if it is not among the ninety-nine, how can it be greater? The response is that it may be outside, and the honor of the ninety-nine is relative to common knowledge, not to the exclusive names.

Key Terms
طبيب
حديث المصابيح
شرح المقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any attribute of Allah found in revelation is a divine name.
Correction: This objection shows that 'tabib' is used descriptively in the hadith, not as a name, so it does not contradict the tawfiq principle.

Checkpoint
What hadith is cited as evidence for the use of 'tabib'?
A) The hadith of the lamps
B) The hadith of the ninety-nine names
C) The hadith of the greatest name
D) The hadith of the Prophet's companions

Key Takeaway
The use of 'tabib' in hadith does not automatically make it a divine name; the distinction between name and description is crucial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet himself described Allah as 'the physician' but scholars say it is not a name, how does that affect the way you understand and use the names of Allah? Are there any names or attributes you use without verifying their source? How can this lesson make you more cautious in your worship?
?
The objection was based on a hadith, yet the answer required deeper knowledge. When you encounter a seemingly clear text that conflicts with scholarly consensus, do you immediately object or seek a deeper understanding? What steps can you follow to emulate the author who referenced an authoritative commentary?
?
This lesson shows that not every description of Allah in revelation is a name. When you call upon Allah, do you ever use words like 'the Physician' without considering whether they are among the 99 names? How will you ensure you use only revealed names in your supplications?

The Term 'Wajib al-Wujud' and Tawfiq
Discusses the permissibility of using 'Wajib al-Wujud'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'Wajib al-Wujud' (Necessary Existence) is permissible to use for Allah despite its absence from scripture, because it is a descriptive phrase, not a name.

Core Idea
Wajib al-Wujud is a description, not a name, so the condition of tawfiq does not apply.

A benefit is noted: some scholars point out that the term 'Wajib al-Wujud' does not appear in the Quran or Sunnah. For those who insist on tawfiq for divine names, this would seem to prohibit its use. However, the passage clarifies that 'Wajib al-Wujud' is actually an attributive phrase (like 'beautiful of face') predicated of something else, not a proper name. Therefore it falls outside the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the benefit
Read the Arabic passage about the term 'Wajib al-Wujud'. Note the objection and the response.
2
Understand the distinction
The response explains that 'Wajib al-Wujud' is a descriptive attribute, not a name. It is like saying 'necessary in existence' which is a description.
3
Apply the principle
Reflect on how this principle can apply to other terms not found in scripture.

Source Text

The Number Ninety-Nine and the Greatest Name
Explains the phrase and discusses the greatest name

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the phrase 'one hundred minus one' as emphasis, and the reasoning behind why the greatest name may be excluded from the ninety-nine.

Core Idea
The ninety-nine names are the well-known ones; the greatest name may be outside them, known only to saints and prophets.

This lesson addresses two interconnected issues. First, the phrase 'one hundred minus one' is explained as an emphasis to prevent misunderstanding of the count. Second, a question arises: if there are more than ninety-nine names, what does the hadith's limitation mean? The answer is that the ninety-nine are those that combine all praiseworthy meanings. Further, the greatest name may not be among them, yet its honor is special.

Guided Path
1
Understand the emphasis
Read the explanation of 'one hundred minus one' as an emphasis and its benefit.
Note the variant reading 'إلا واحدة'.
2
Grapple with the limitation
Read the question: if there are more names, what does the hadith's 'ninety-nine' mean? Note the answer: the ninety-nine are those that combine all praiseworthy meanings.
3
Consider the greatest name
Read the question about the greatest name and the response that it may be outside the ninety-nine, known only to a select few.

The response is that it may be outside, and the honor of the ninety-nine is relative to common knowledge, not to the exclusive names.

Correction: The passage allows for other names, including the greatest name which may be outside the ninety-nine.

Rejection of the Name al-‘Āqil
The author rejects the name al-‘āqil by analyzing its root.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the name 'al-‘āqil' is not suitable for Allah due to its implication of potential wrongdoing.

Core Idea
Divine names must be free of any implication of deficiency.

The author begins by examining specific names that might seem positive but actually imply limitations. The first is 'al-‘āqil' (the intelligent), which carries a hidden meaning of restraint against wrongdoing. This sets the stage for a crucial principle: attributes of Allah must be absolutely perfect, with no hint of imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-‘āqil
Read the Arabic and translation for the section on al-‘āqil. Notice the author's definition of 'aql as a hindrance.
2
Analyze the implication
Consider: if 'aql prevents one from doing wrong, it implies that without it, wrong could be done. This is a deficiency.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself why any name that suggests a prior possibility of deficiency is rejected for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا لفظ العاقل لأن العقل علم مانع عن الإقدام على ما لا ينبغي مأخوذ من العقال، وإنما يتصور هذا المعنى فيمن يدعوه الداعي إلى ما لا ينبغي، ولا لفظ الفطن لأن الفطانة سرعة إدراك ما يراد تعريضه على السامع فتكون مسبوقة بالجهل، ولا لفظ الطبيب لأن الطب يراد به علم مأخوذ من التجارب إلى غير ذلك من الأسماء التي فيها نوع إيهام بما لا يصح في حقه تعالى. وقد يقال: بد من نفي ذلك الإيهام مع الإشعار بالتعظيم حتى يصح الإطلاق بلا توقيف، وذهب الشيخ ومتابعوه إلى أنه لا بد من التوقيف وهو المختار ( وذلك للاحتياط احترازاً عما يوهم باطلاً لعظم الخطر في ذلك فلا يجوز الاكتفاء في عدم إيهام الباطل بمبلغ إدراكنا بل لا بد من الاستناد إلى إذن الشرع ). ثم قال: ولا لفظ الطبيب. اعترض عليه بأنه قد ورد في حديث المصابيح أنه عليه السلام قال لمن قال: إني طبيب: أنت رفيق والله الطبيب، وقد عرفت مما نقلناه عن شرح المقاصد جوابه.
And not the word 'al-āqil' because 'aql' is a knowledge that prevents one from embarking on what is not fitting, derived from 'al-‘iqāl' (the hobble), and this meaning is only conceivable in one whom the caller invites to what is not fitting; and not the word 'al-fatīn' because 'fatāna' is quickness of perception of what is intended to be presented to the listener, so it is preceded by ignorance; and not the word 'al-ṭabīb' because 'ṭibb' is meant as knowledge derived from experience, and so on for other names that contain a kind of illusion of what is not correct regarding Him, Exalted. And it may be said: instead of negating that illusion, with indication of exaltation, so that [their] use without tawqīf would be valid. But the Shaykh and his followers held that tawqīf is necessary, and that is the chosen view — for caution, to avoid anything that suggests falsehood, because of the great danger in that; so it is not sufficient to rely on our own perception of the absence of false suggestion; rather, reliance on the permission of the Law is necessary. Then he said: 'And not the word 'al-ṭabīb'.' An objection was raised against it because there occurs in the hadith of the lamps that he (peace be upon him) said to the one who said 'I am a physician': 'You are a kind companion, and Allah is the Physician.' And you have known from what we transmitted from Sharh al-Maqāṣid the answer to it.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. The author explains why certain names cannot be used for Allah without tawqīf (revelation). He argues that names like al-‘āqil, al-fatīn, al-ṭabīb, though seemingly positive, carry implications that are not fitting for Allah. For example, al-‘āqil (the intelligent) derives from 'aql which means a hindrance, implying that without it one would do wrong — this is not applicable to Allah who is inherently perfect. Similarly, al-fatīn (the perceptive) implies prior ignorance. Al-ṭabīb (the physician) implies knowledge from experience, which is not appropriate for Allah. The author then mentions a view that if these names are accompanied by an understanding that negates such implications, they could be valid without tawqīf. However, the correct view (mukhtār) is that tawqīf is necessary as a precaution, because the danger of attributing something false to Allah is great, and we cannot rely on our own judgment alone; we need explicit permission from revelation. Then he addresses an objection: the Prophet himself used the word 'ṭabīb' for Allah in a hadith. The answer, referenced from Sharh al-Maqāṣid, is presumably that the Prophet used it with proper understanding and under divine guidance, not as a general license for everyone to use it without tawqīf.

Key Terms
العاقل
العقل
العقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the name 'al-‘āqil' considered unsuitable for Allah?
A) It is a name used only for humans.
B) It implies that Allah needed a hindrance from wrongdoing.
C) It is not mentioned in the Qur'an.
D) It suggests that Allah learns over time.

Key Takeaway
Names for Allah must not carry even a hint of deficiency; 'al-‘āqil' implies a need for restraint, so it is rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that Allah needs no restraint challenge your own assumptions about His attributes? Do you ever unconsciously think of Allah as having human-like limitations?
?
If a name like 'al-‘Āqil' is rejected for suggesting deficiency, what does that demand from you when you describe Allah? Are you careful to only use names and descriptions that come from revelation?
?
Reflect on your own need for 'aql as a restraining force against evil. How does this contrast with Allah's absolute perfection? What should this comparison inspire in your worship?

The Necessity of Tawqīf and the Objection on al-Ṭabīb
The author argues for tawqīf and answers an objection about the hadith of al-Ṭabīb.

Learning Objective

Core Idea

The Hadith of 99 Names and the Meaning of Iḥṣā
The author explains the hadith of 99 names, their specification, and the meaning of Iḥṣā.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the hadith of 99 names, the meaning of 'mashhur', and the correct interpretation of the numerical limitation.

Core Idea
The 99 names highlight the most excellent names, not all names.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif discusses the famous hadith about Allah's 99 names. The author clarifies that the hadith does not limit Allah's names to 99, but rather describes those that are most excellent. He also explains the meaning of 'ihsā' and why the number is specified as 'one hundred except one'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall flow.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the hadith citation
Find the hadith from the two Sahīhs. Note that the Sahīhs do not list the names themselves, but other scholars specified them.
The phrase 'in the well-known' hints at other transmitted names.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The author presents an objection: if more names exist, why the number 99? Then he answers that the hadith is descriptive, not restrictive. The 99 are the most excellent.
This is the key theological point.
4
Analyze the phrase 'one hundred except one'
Understand that this emphasis prevents misunderstandings about the exact number. Note the variant feminine form.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write down the main takeaway: the hadith does not restrict Allah's names; it points to the most excellent ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي ورد به التوقيف في المشهور تسعة اسماء تسعة وتسعون، فقد ورد في الصحيحين أن لله تسعة وتسعين اسماً، مائة إلا واحداً من أحصاها دخل الجنة. ليس فيهما تعيين تلك الأسماء لكن الترمذي والبيهقي عيناها كما في الكتاب وإنما قال: في (المشهور) إذ قد ورد التوقيف بغيرها. قوله: (إذ قد ورد التوقيف بغيرها إلخ) فإن قلت: إذا كانت أسماء الله تعالى زائدة على التسع والتسعين فما معنى الحصر المستفاد من الحديث المنقول من صحيح البخاري ومسلم؟ قلت: قوله عليه السلام: من أحصاها دخل الجنة في موقع الصفة لقوله: تسعة وتسعين اسماً ثم إن أسماء الله تعالى يجوز أن تتفاوت فضيلتها لتفاوت معانيها في الجلالة والشرف فيكون تسعة وتسعون اسماً منها بأعيانها يجمع أنواعاً من المعاني المنبئة عن الجلال لا يجمع ذلك غيرها. قوله: (مئة إلا واحداً) تأكيد لقوله تسعة وتسعين قيل: وفائدته دفع توهم أن العبارة سبعة وتسعين أو سبعين وفي بعض الروايات إلا واحدة فتأنيث واحدة على تأويل الاسم بالكلمة.
And what has been transmitted by tawqīf in the well-known (mashhūr) is nine names, ninety-nine. It has been transmitted in the two Sahīhs that Allah has ninety-nine names, one hundred except one; whoever enumerates them will enter Paradise. In them there is no specification of those names, but al-Tirmidhī and al-Bayhaqī specified them as in the book. And he only said: 'in the well-known' because tawqīf has occurred for others besides them. His statement: 'since tawqīf has occurred for others besides them etc.' For if you say: If the names of Allah exceed ninety-nine, what is the meaning of limitation derived from the hadith narrated from Sahīh al-Bukhārī and Muslim? I say: His statement (peace be upon him) 'whoever enumerates them will enter Paradise' is in the position of an adjective for his statement 'ninety-nine names'. Then, the names of Allah may differ in virtue due to the difference of their meanings in majesty and honor; thus there are ninety-nine names among them that combine types of meanings indicative of majesty which others do not combine. His statement: 'one hundred except one' is an emphasis on his statement 'ninety-nine'. It is said: its benefit is to repel the misunderstanding that the expression is ninety-seven or seventy. And in some narrations 'except one' (with feminine ending), the feminization of 'one' on the interpretation of 'name' as 'word'.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation. The author begins by stating that the well-known names revealed by tawqīf are ninety-nine, citing the hadith from the two Sahīhs. He notes that the Sahīhs do not list them, but al-Tirmidhī and al-Bayhaqī did. The phrase 'in the well-known' indicates that other names have also been revealed. He then addresses an objection: if there are more than ninety-nine names, what is the meaning of the numerical limitation in the hadith? The answer is that the hadith is descriptive, not restrictive; the ninety-nine are the most excellent names that combine superior meanings of majesty, and others do not have that combination. He also explains the phrase 'one hundred except one' as an emphasis to avoid misunderstanding that the number could be ninety-seven or seventy, and notes the variant with feminine 'wāḥidah' due to interpreting 'ism' as 'kalimah'.

Correction: The author clarifies that the hadith is not restrictive; other names exist, but the 99 are the most excellent.

The Term 'Wājib al-Wujūd' and Tawqīf
A note on the term 'wājib al-wujūd' not occurring in Quran or Sunnah and its permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the issue of the term 'wājib al-wujūd' not being found in revelation and how it is permissible as a descriptive attribute rather than a name requiring tawqīf.

Core Idea
Wājib al-wujūd is a description, not a revealed name, so it avoids the tawqīf requirement.

This passage addresses a common concern among students of theology: the term 'wājib al-wujūd' (Necessary of Existence) is widely used in kalam, yet it does not appear in the Quran or hadith. Does this violate the principle of tawqīf (that divine names must be revealed)? The author offers a subtle resolution that preserves the permissibility of this important term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's phrase 'Here is a benefit that ought to be pointed out' — this signals an important clarification.
2
Identify the problem
The problem: the term 'wājib al-wujūd' is not in the Quran or Sunnah. For those who require tawqīf, using it for Allah would be problematic. Understand why this is a significant issue in Islamic theology.
3
Understand the resolution
The author suggests that 'wājib al-wujūd' is actually a descriptive phrase (ṣifa) meaning 'whose existence is necessary', similar to 'ḥasan al-wajh'. Such descriptions are not subject to tawqīf. Reflect on how this distinction solves the problem.
This is a key nuance: names vs. attributes in the tawqīf debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وذهب الشيخ ومتابعوه إلخ) هاهنا فائدة ينبغي أن ينبه عليها وهو أن بعض الفضلاء صرح بأن لفظ واجب الوجود لم يرد في الكتاب والسنة فمن اشترط التوقيف في أسماء الله تعالى ينبغي أن لا يجوز إطلاقه عليه تعالى مع أن هذا الإطلاق شائع، اللهم إلا أن يقال: هذا في التحقيق صفة جرت على غير من هي له إذ المعنى واجب وجوده كما في حسن الوجه فليس من محل النزاع فليتأمل.
His statement: 'and the Shaykh and his followers went etc.' Here is a benefit that ought to be pointed out: that some virtuous people explicitly stated that the expression 'wājib al-wujūd' (Necessary of Existence) does not occur in the Book and the Sunnah. So whoever stipulates tawqīf for the names of Allah Most High should not permit using it for Him, even though this usage is widespread. Unless it is said: this, in reality, is an attribute that has been applied to other than what it is originally for, because the meaning is 'whose existence is necessary', like 'ḥasan al-wajh' (beautiful of face), so it is not within the domain of dispute. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a clarification (fāʾida). The author notes that some scholars have pointed out that the term 'wājib al-wujūd' (Necessary Being) is not found in the Quran or hadith. Therefore, those who require tawqīf for divine names would logically not permit its use for Allah. However, the author suggests a way out: this term is actually a descriptive phrase (ṣifa) used in a derived sense, meaning 'whose existence is necessary', similar to expressions like 'ḥasan al-wajh' (beautiful of face). Such descriptive phrases are not the subject of the dispute over names. So the widespread usage may be acceptable.

Key Terms

الكتاب والسنة
توقيف
صفة

Correction: The passage clarifies that it is a descriptive attribute (ṣifa), not a name, and thus not subject to tawqīf. It is permissible as a description.

Checkpoint

A) It is a name from the 99.
B) It is a description like 'ḥasan al-wajh'.
C) It is forbidden.
D) It is a name from the hadith.

Key Takeaway
The term 'wājib al-wujūd' is a permissible descriptive attribute, not a revealed name, thus avoiding the tawqīf objection.

The Greatest Name: Inclusion in the 99 and Spiritual Affinity
Author addresses inclusion of the greatest name and its true condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly opinions on whether the greatest name of Allah is among the 99 names and why its power depends on spiritual affinity rather than mere utterance.

Core Idea
The greatest name's power lies in spiritual harmony with Allah, not in a secret word.

A common question in Islamic theology is whether the greatest name (ism al-aʿẓam) is among the 99 beautiful names. This passage presents an objection, two possible answers, and a profound insight from al-Ghazālī that reorients our understanding: the key is not the word itself but the inner transformation and connection with Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The objector presents a dilemma: if the greatest name is not among the 99, how can the 99 be honored? If it is, why is it hidden?
Notice the precise phrasing of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two possible answers
The author offers two possibilities: the greatest name is either outside the 99 or included but ambiguous. Understand the reasoning behind each.
3
Grasp the deeper truth about spiritual affinity
Al-Ghazālī's teaching: the power of the greatest name (and any name) depends on achieving munāsaba (spiritual affinity) and acquiring Allah's attributes. Mere recitation is not enough.
This is the key takeaway.
4
Apply the lesson to your own practice
Reflect on how this shifts focus from seeking a 'magic word' to cultivating a character that reflects Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: اسم الله تعالى الأعظم لم يدخل في التسعة والتسعين، فكيف يختص بمزيد الشرف ما هو خارج عنها وإن دخل فكيف ذلك وهي مشهورة والاسم الأعظم يختص بمعرفته ولِيّ أو نبي وهي سبب كرامات عظيمة لمن عرفه؟ قلت: يحتمل الخروج ويكون شرف هذه الأسماء المعدودة بالإضافة إلى الأسماء المشهورة عند الجماهير لا بالإضافة إلى الأسماء التي يختص بمعرفتها الأنبياء والأولياء ويحتمل الدخول لكنه مبهم لا يعرفه بعينه الأولياء، ومنشأ الكرامات هو الدعاء به بخصوصه. كذا ذكره الإمام الغزالي في شرح الأسماء الحسنى والحق أن التصرف بالاسم الأعظم وبأي اسم كان من أسماء التصرف موقوف على تحصيل المناسبة مع مسماه والتخلق بأخلاقه وبمجرد ذكر الاسم الأعظم لا يحصل المناسبة كما أن ذكر اسم السلطان لا ينفع ما لم يحصل معه المناسبة الخاصة. اللهم انفعنا.
For if you say: The greatest name of Allah (ism Allāh al-aʿẓam) is not included among the ninety-nine, then how can it be singled out for increased honor what is outside them? And if it is included, how is that, since they are well-known and the greatest name is known only to a prophet or saint, and it is the cause of great miracles for whoever knows it? I say: It is possible that it is outside, and the honor of these counted names is relative to the names well-known among the masses, not relative to the names which are known specifically to prophets and saints. And it is possible that it is included, but it is ambiguous (mubham), and the saints do not know it in its specific identity. The origin of miracles is supplication with it specifically. Thus al-Ghazālī mentioned it in his commentary on al-Asmāʾ al-Ḥusnā. And the truth is that the power to act with the greatest name, or with any name among the names of action, is dependent on obtaining a spiritual affinity (munāsaba) with its named one and acquiring His attributes. Mere mention of the greatest name does not achieve this affinity, just as the mention of the sultan's name does not benefit unless there is a special connection with him. O Allah, benefit us.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector raises a dilemma: if the greatest name (ism al-aʿẓam) is not among the 99, then how can the 99 be considered specially honored? If it is among them, then why is it hidden and known only to saints? The author gives two possible answers: first, the greatest name may be outside the 99, and the honor of the 99 is relative to the common well-known names, not to the special names known only to prophets. Second, the greatest name may be among the 99 but ambiguous (mubham), not specifically known. The real power of the greatest name, according to al-Ghazālī, is not in the mere utterance but in achieving spiritual affinity (munāsaba) with Allah and acquiring His attributes, like the sultan's name being useless without a special connection.

Key Terms
الاسم الأعظم
الأنبياء
الأولياء
الكرامات
المناسبة
التخلق بأخلاقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the ism al-aʿẓam is a magical phrase that works by mere recitation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that its power requires spiritual affinity (munāsaba) and acquiring Allah's attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what does the power of the greatest name depend?
A) The number of times it is recited.
B) Knowing the specific word.
C) Achieving spiritual affinity with Allah and acquiring His attributes.
D) Being a prophet or saint.

Key Takeaway
The greatest name's power requires inner transformation and closeness to Allah, not just verbal repetition.

Additional Names of Allah from Quran and Hadith
The author lists additional divine names from Quran and hadith and defines iḥṣā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that many more names of Allah are found in the Quran and hadith beyond the famous 99, and what it means to enumerate them (iḥṣā').

Core Idea
The 99 names are a basis, but Allah's names are many more in revelation.

The author expands on the well-known list of 99 names by citing additional names from the Quran and hadith. He then clarifies what the Prophet meant by 'enumerate' (iḥṣā') the names — it is not mere counting but deep understanding and devotion. This lesson surveys those additional names and the true meaning of iḥṣā'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic list of names from Quran and hadith, then the translation. Let the author's own enumeration come through.
2
Identify the sources
Notice the three categories: names from the Quran, from hadith, and additional ones from the narration of Ibn Mājah.
This classification is key to seeing the breadth of revealed names.
3
Understand iḥṣā'
The author gives two meanings: simple memorization or comprehensive mastery. Reflect on which one leads to Paradise.
The second meaning includes knowledge, faith, and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما في القرآن فكالمولى والنصير والغالب والقاهر والقريب والرب والناصر والأعلى والأكرم وأحسن الخالقين وأرحم الراحمين وذي الطول وذي القوة وذي المعارج إلى غير ذلك. وأما في الحديث فكالحنان والمنّان، وقد ورد في رواية ابن ماجه أسماء ليست في الرواية المشهورة كالتام والقديم والوتر والشديد والكافي وغيرها. وإحصاؤها إما حفظها لأنه إنما يحصل بتكرار مجموعنا وتعدادها مراراً، وإما ضبطها حصراً وتعداداً وعلماً وإيماناً وقياماً بحقوقها. وبالجملة ( فلتخصها إحصاء) طمعاً في دخول الجنة. قوله: (وأما في الحديث إلخ) اختلف في أنه هل يجوز تسمية الله تعالى بما ثبت بخبر الواحدة فقيل: يجوز لأنه من باب العمل وقيل لا يجوز لأنه من باب الاعتقاد ثم الظاهر أن الإطلاق وإن كان على وجه الإخبار يكفي في الأذن ما لم يكن في السوق أمر آخر يمنعه وإلا يلزم أن لا يكون كثير من الأسماء مأذونة فلا عبرة بما في شرح المشارق من أنه لا يلزم من قول عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها أن الله رفيق يحب الرفق صحة أن يقال: يا رفيق لأنه ذكر على وجه الإخبار. قوله: (فكالحنان) هو بالتشديد من الحنان بالتخفيف وهو الرحمة قال الله تعالى {وحناناً من لدنا} [مريم: 13].
As for in the Quran, [there are names] like al-Mawlā, al-Naṣīr, al-Ghālib, al-Qāhir, al-Qarīb, al-Rabb, al-Nāṣir, al-Aʿlā, al-Akram, Aḥsan al-Khāliqīn, Arḥam al-Rāḥimīn, Dhī al-Ṭawl, Dhī al-Quwwa, Dhī al-Maʿārij, and so forth. And as for in the hadith, [there are names] like al-Ḥannān and al-Mannān. It has occurred in the narration of Ibn Mājah names not in the well-known narration, like al-Tāmm, al-Qadīm, al-Witr, al-Shadīd, al-Kāfī, and others. And their enumeration (iḥṣā') is either memorizing them, because it is only achieved by repeating our total and counting them repeatedly, or it is mastering them, counting, knowledge, faith, and fulfilling their rights. In short, 'so let them specialize in enumeration' hoping for entry into Paradise. His statement: 'And as for in the hadith etc.' There is disagreement about whether it is permissible to name Allah Most High with what is established by a single-narrator report (khabar wāḥid). Some said: it is permissible because it falls under action; some said: it is not permissible because it falls under belief. Then the apparent view is that the use, even if in the manner of reporting (ikhbār), suffices for permission as long as there is no other impediment in the market; otherwise, many names would not be permitted. So no weight is given to what is in Sharḥ al-Mashāriq that from ʿĀʾisha's statement (may Allah be pleased with her) that 'Allah is gentle (rafīq) and loves gentleness' does not entail the correctness of saying 'Yā Rafīq' because it was mentioned in the manner of reporting. His statement: 'like al-Ḥannān' is with tashdīd from al-ḥann (with tashdīd? actually from al-ḥanān with takhfīf), which is mercy, as Allah said {and tenderness from Us} [Maryam: 13].
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion. The author lists additional names of Allah found in the Quran (e.g., al-Mawlā, al-Naṣīr) and in hadith (e.g., al-Ḥannān, al-Mannān), as well as names from Ibn Mājah's narration (e.g., al-Tāmm, al-Qadīm). He then explains that 'iḥṣā' (enumeration of the 99 names) means either memorizing them or fully grasping them with knowledge, faith, and practice. Then he discusses a debated issue: is it permissible to use names for Allah that are established only by khabar wāḥid (single-narrator hadith)? Some say yes, because it is a matter of practice (ʿamal) not belief; others say no, because it concerns belief (iʿtiqād). The author leans toward permissibility for the purpose of addressing Allah (ikhbār), provided no other obstacle prevents it; otherwise, many names would be prohibited. He dismisses the view from Sharḥ al-Mashāriq that ʿĀʾisha's statement 'Allah is rafīq' does not permit calling Him 'Yā Rafīq' since it was merely informative. He clarifies that al-Ḥannān means mercy.

إحصاء

المنان

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author shows that many other names are found in the Quran and hadith, so the 99 names are not an exhaustive list.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two meanings of enumerating (iḥṣā') Allah's names?
A) Memorizing them and understanding their meanings.
B) Memorizing them and mastering them through knowledge, faith, and practice.
C) Counting them and writing them down.
D) Repeating them in prayer and teaching them.

Key Takeaway
The revealed names of Allah are not limited to the famous 99; many more are found in the Quran and hadith, and enumerating them requires both memorization and deep devotion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Using Divine Names from Single-Narrator Reports
The author discusses the permissibility of using divine names from single-narrator reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether names of Allah derived from khabar wāḥid can be used, and the author's preferred view.

Core Idea
Using a divine name from a single-narrator report is permissible as a matter of action, unless there is a specific impediment.

After listing additional names from hadith, the author turns to a methodological question: Can we use a name for Allah that is reported only in a single-narrator hadith (khabar wāḥid)? This lesson examines the two scholarly positions and the author's reasoning, including a critique of a stricter view from Sharḥ al-Mashāriq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The author presents the disagreement about using names from khabar wāḥid. Note that this is a methodological debate among scholars.
2
Understand the two positions
One group says permissible (action-based), the other says not permissible (belief-based). Reflect on why each position takes its stance.
The key distinction is between ʿamal (action) and iʿtiqād (belief).
3
Analyze the author's leaning
The author states that the apparent view is that use in the manner of reporting is sufficient for permission, unless there is an impediment. This suggests he favors permissibility.
He argues that otherwise many commonly accepted names would be prohibited.
4
Review the counterexample
The author dismisses the argument that ʿĀʾisha's statement cannot justify saying 'Yā Rafīq'. Note his reasoning: it was mentioned informatively, not as a prohibition.

As for in the Quran, [there are names] like al-Mawlā, al-Naṣīr, al-Ghālib, al-Qāhir, al-Qarīb, al-Rabb, al-Nāṣir, al-Aʿlā, al-Akram, Aḥsan al-Khāliqīn, Arḥam al-Rāḥimīn, Dhī al-Ṭawl, Dhī al-Quwwa, Dhī al-Maʿārij, and so forth. His statement: 'And as for in the hadith etc.' There is disagreement about whether it is permissible to name Allah Most High with what is established by a single-narrator report (khabar wāḥid). Some said: it is permissible because it falls under action; some said: it is not permissible because it falls under belief. Then the apparent view is that the use, even if in the manner of reporting (ikhbār), suffices for permission as long as there is no other impediment in the market; otherwise, many names would not be permitted. So no weight is given to what is in Sharḥ al-Mashāriq that from ʿĀʾisha's statement (may Allah be pleased with her) that 'Allah is gentle (rafīq) and loves gentleness' does not entail the correctness of saying 'Yā Rafīq' because it was mentioned in the manner of reporting. His statement: 'like al-Ḥannān' is with tashdīd from al-ḥann (with tashdīd? actually from al-ḥanān with takhfīf), which is mercy, as Allah said {and tenderness from Us} [Maryam: 13].
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion. The author lists additional names of Allah found in the Quran (e.g., al-Mawlā, al-Naṣīr) and in hadith (e.g., al-Ḥannān, al-Mannān), as well as names from Ibn Mājah's narration (e.g., al-Tāmm, al-Qadīm). He then explains that 'iḥṣā' (enumeration of the 99 names) means either memorizing them or fully grasping them with knowledge, faith, and practice. Then he discusses a debated issue: is it permissible to use names for Allah that are established only by khabar wāḥid (single-narrator hadith)? Some say yes, because it is a matter of practice (ʿamal) not belief; others say no, because it concerns belief (iʿtiqād). The author leans toward permissibility for the purpose of addressing Allah (ikhbār), provided no other obstacle prevents it; otherwise, many names would be prohibited. He dismisses the view from Sharḥ al-Mashāriq that ʿĀʾisha's statement 'Allah is rafīq' does not permit calling Him 'Yā Rafīq' since it was merely informative. He clarifies that al-Ḥannān means mercy.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد
الإطلاق
الاعتقاد
العمل
الحنان
المنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any name for Allah found in a hadith, even a single-narrator report, is automatically permissible to use as a divine name.
Correction: The passage shows there is a debate; the author's view is that it is permissible as long as there is no impediment, but not all scholars agree.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that using names from khabar wāḥid is permissible?
A) Because it is a matter of belief.
B) Because it is a matter of practice, and there is no other impediment.
C) Because all ahadith are authentic.
D) Because the Prophet always used them.

Key Takeaway
The use of divine names from single-narrator reports is a matter of scholarly debate, with the author favoring permissibility for non-ritual use unless a specific impediment exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by a name, do you ever stop to ask yourself where that name came from and whether it is authentically attributed to Him? The lesson shows that even a single report can make a name permissible to use—but are you using any names that have no basis at all? Take one divine name you use regularly and research its source in the Quran or Sunnah today.
?
The author refutes the idea that a name mentioned only in a report cannot be used in prayer. Have you ever avoided using a beautiful name of Allah because you were unsure if it was 'authentic'? What would it take for you to be confident? This lesson invites you to trust the scholars who have permitted such names—but also to verify their reasoning. Will you remain passive, or will you actively learn the evidence for the names you love?
?
Consider the example of 'ar-Rafiq' from 'Aisha's report. She simply described Allah as gentle; the author says that itself is enough to address Him as 'O Gentle One.' Does your heart tremble with awe when you use such a name, or has it become a mere habit? Resolve today to pronounce each name of Allah with the reverence it deserves, and to learn its meaning so that your supplication is not empty words.

The Name 'Allāh': Proper Noun View
al-Ījī introduces the name 'Allāh' and states the proper noun view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position that 'Allāh' is a proper noun (ʿalam jāmid) with no derivation, as held by notable scholars.

Core Idea
Some scholars hold that 'Allāh' is a unique name with no root.

In this passage, al-Ījī begins by defining 'Allāh' as a name specific to Allah alone. He then presents the first major opinion: that it is a proper noun, not derived from any other word. This view is attributed to linguistic authorities like al-Khalīl and Sībawayh, and jurists like Abū Ḥanīfa and al-Shāfiʿī.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining 'Allāh'
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the opening statement that 'Allāh' is a name specific to Allah.
Notice the phrase 'اسم خاص بذاته'.
2
Identify the first opinion and its proponents
The passage states that some say it is a proper noun (علم جامد) with no derivation. List the scholars mentioned: al-Khalīl, Sībawayh, Abū Ḥanīfa, al-Shāfiʿī, al-Khaṭṭābī, and al-Ghazālī.
This is one of the two opinions of al-Khalīl and Sībawayh.
3
Consider why this matters in kalām
If 'Allāh' is a proper noun, it is not derived from any attribute, emphasizing its uniqueness. This has implications for theological discussions about Allah's names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: (الله) وهو اسم خاص بذاته لا يوصف به غيره أي لا يطلق على غيره أصلاً. فقيل: هو علم جامد لا اشتقاق له، وهو أحد قولي الخليل وسيبويه، والمروي عن أبي حنيفة والشافعي وأبي سليمان الخطابي والغزالي رحمهم الله تعالى. وقيل: مشتق وأصله الإله حذفت الهمزة لنقلها وأدغم اللام من أله إذا تعبد. وهو المراد بقوله: (إذا تعبد). وقيل: الإله مأخوذ من الوله وهو الحيرة ومرجعهما صفة إضافية هي كونه معبوداً للخلائق ومختاراً للعقول. (وقيل: معنى الإله هو) اسم، قوله: (وأصله الإله) يحتمل أن يكون حرف التعريف فيه من المحكي وهو الظاهر من كلام الشارح ويحتمل أن يكون من الحكاية بأن يكون الأصل لها منكراً. وقد ذهب إلى كل فرقة. قوله: (من الوله) فإله على كلا الوجهين فعال بمعنى مفعول لكن على الثاني أصله ولاه أبدلت الواو همزة كما قالوا في وساد أساد. وحكى ابن جني أن سيبويه رؤي في منامه فقيل: ما فعل الله تعالى بك؟ فقال: خيراً وذكر كرامة عظيمة فقيل له: بم نلت ذاك؟ فقال: لقولي أن الله أعرف المعارف.
So we say: 'Allāh' is a name specific to His Essence, not used for other than Him; i.e., it is never applied to other than Him. So it is said: It is a proper noun (ʿalam jāmid) with no derivation, and this is one of the two opinions of al-Khalīl and Sībawayh, and reported from Abū Ḥanīfa, al-Shāfiʿī, Abū Sulaymān al-Khaṭṭābī, and al-Ghazālī, may Allah have mercy on them. And it is said: It is derived, and its origin is al-ilāh, the hamza was deleted due to transfer and the lām was assimilated from 'aliha' if he worshiped (iʿtabada). That is meant by his statement 'if he worshiped'. And it is said: al-ilāh is taken from al-walah which is bewilderment, and both of these refer to an attributive quality (ṣifa iḍāfiyya) which is His being worshiped by creation and preferred by intellects. (And it is said: the meaning of al-ilāh is) a name. His statement: 'and its origin is al-ilāh' might mean that the definite article in it is from what is related (al-maḥkī), which is apparent from the commentator's words, and it might mean that it is from the narrative (al-ḥikāya) such that the original is indefinite. Each group has adopted a position. His statement: 'from al-walah' so 'ilāh' on both faces is faʿʿāl in the sense of mafʿūl (object), but according to the second origin it is walah, the wāw was replaced with hamza, as they said for wisād: asād. And Ibn Jinnī reported that Sībawayh was seen in a dream and it was said: What did Allah do with you? He said: Good, and mentioned a great honor. It was said to him: By what did you attain that? He said: Due to my saying that Allah is the most known of known things (aʿraf al-maʿārif).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author begins by stating that 'Allāh' is a name specific to Allah, never used for others. He then presents two main opinions on its derivation. The first opinion holds that it is a proper noun (ʿalam jāmid) with no derivation, attributed to al-Khalīl, Sībawayh, Abū Ḥanīfa, al-Shāfiʿī, al-Khaṭṭābī, and al-Ghazālī. The second opinion holds that it is derived from al-ilāh, either from the root 'aliha' meaning to worship (ʿibāda) or from 'waliha' meaning to be bewildered (ḥayra). Both derivations lead to an attributive quality (ṣifa iḍāfiyya): Allah being worshipped and bewildering intellects. The author also discusses the grammatical analysis of the definite article in 'al-ilāh'—whether it is original or narrative. He notes that Sībawayh had a dream where his correct belief that Allah is 'aʿraf al-maʿārif' (most known) earned him honor.

Key Terms
الله
علم جامد
اشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'Allāh' is simply a contraction of 'al-ilāh' without nuance.
Correction: The first opinion emphasizes that 'Allāh' is a proper noun with no derivation, like a name that does not come from any verb or noun.

Checkpoint
What is the first opinion on the name 'Allāh' according to the passage?
A) It is derived from al-ilāh meaning worship.
B) It is a proper noun with no derivation.
C) It is derived from al-walah meaning bewilderment.
D) It is a composite name.

Key Takeaway
The first opinion holds that 'Allāh' is a proper noun without derivation, supported by prominent linguists and jurists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you utter 'Allāh', do you realize that this name is so unique that it has no derivation? How does this deepen your awe and reverence in every prayer?
?
The scholars quoted devoted their lives to safeguarding the uniqueness of Allah's name. In what ways might you unconsciously dilute that uniqueness by associating others with Him in your heart or actions?
?
If 'Allāh' is a proper noun with no root, it means He is not defined by any created concept. Will you let this truth transform your understanding of His transcendence, starting today?

The Derived View of 'Allāh': From al-Ilāh to Worship and Bewilderment
al-Ījī presents the derived opinion and its two variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that 'Allāh' is derived from 'al-ilāh', either from the root meaning worship or bewilderment, and the grammatical analysis involved.

Core Idea
The name 'Allāh' may be derived from al-ilāh, pointing to worship or bewildering intellects.

After presenting the proper noun view, the author turns to the second opinion: that 'Allāh' is derived from 'al-ilāh'. He explores two possible roots—ʿaliha (to worship) and waliha (to be bewildered)—and discusses the grammatical transformation of the definite article. This lesson unpacks these linguistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the derived opinion
Read the Arabic and translation for this part, focusing on the two derivations and the grammar discussion.
Notice the terms 'مشتق' and 'الإله'.
2
Understand the two proposed roots: worship and bewilderment
Identify the two roots: one from 'aliha' (to worship) and one from 'waliha' (to be bewildered). Both lead to an attributive quality (ṣifa iḍāfiyya).
The text says 'مرجعهما صفة إضافية'.
3
Examine the grammar of the definite article and the dream story
The author discusses whether the 'al-' in 'al-ilāh' is original (maḥkī) or narrative (ḥikāya). Then, the dream of Sībawayh shows that 'Allāh' is 'aʿraf al-maʿārif' (most known).
The dream indicates the high status of this knowledge.

The Principle of Tawfiqi Names and Their Enumeration
Introduces the tawfiqi nature of divine names with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the names of Allah are based on revelation (tawfiqi) and the importance of enumerating them as a means to Paradise.

Core Idea
God’s names are not invented; they are received through revelation.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle in Islamic theology: the names of Allah are derived from revelation, not human reasoning. The passage lists examples from the Quran and Hadith and emphasizes the virtue of enumerating the divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the principle of tawfiqi names.
2
Identify the examples
Note the examples from the Quran and Hadith, such as al-Mawla, al-Nasir, al-Hannan, etc.
These are not exhaustive but illustrative.
3
Understand the meaning of ihsa'
Ihsa' (enumeration) is explained as preserving or controlling the names, and the passage relates it to entering Paradise.
This shows the spiritual importance of learning the names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السابع - المقصد الثالث : تسميته تعالى بالاسماء توفيقية
أما في القرآن فكالمولى والنصير والغالب والقاهر والقريب والرب والناصر والأعلى والأكرم وأحسن الخالقين وأرحم الراحمين وذي الطول وذي القوة وذي المعارج إلى غير ذلك . وأما في الحديث فكالحنان والمنّان، وقد ورد في رواية ابن ماجه أسماء ليست في الرواية المشهورة كالتام والقديم والوتر والشديد والكافي وغيرها، وإحصاؤها إما حفظها لأنه إنما يحصل بتكرار مجموعنا وتعدادها مراراً، وإما ضبطها حصراً وتعداداً وعلماً وإيماناً وقياماً بحقوقها. وبالجملة ( فلتخصها إحصاء) طمعاً في دخول الجنة . فنقول : ( الله ) وهو (اسم خاص بذاته لا يوصف به غيره ) أي لا يطلق على غيره أصلاً. ( فقيل : ) هو ( علم جامد ( لا اشتقاق له، وهو أحد قولي الخليل وسيبويه، والمروي عن أبي حنيفة والشافعي وأبي سليمان الخطابي والغزالي رحمهم الله تعالى. وقيل: مشتق وأصله الإله حذفت الهمزة لنقلها وأدغم اللام وهو من أله ) بفتح اللام أي عبد وهو المراد بقوله : (إذا تعبد . وقيل : ( الإله مأخوذ ( من الوله وهو الحيرة ومرجعهما صفة إضافية هي كونه معبوداً للخلائق ومختاراً للعقول . (وقيل: ) معنى الإله (هو ) بمعرفتها الأنبياء والأولياء ويحتمل الدخول لكنه مبهم لا يعرفه بعينه الأولى، ومنشأ الكرامات هو الدعاء به بخصوصه كذا ذكره الإمام الغزالي في شرح الأسماء الحسنى والحق أن التصرف بالاسم الأعظم وبأي اسم كان من أسماء التصرف موقوف على تحصيل المناسبة مع مسماه والتخلق بأخلاقه وبمجرد ذكر الاسم الأعظم لا يحصل المناسبة كما أن ذكر اسم السلطان لا ينفع ما لم يحصل معه المناسبة الخاصة اللهم انفعنا .
قوله : ( وأما في الحديث إلخ) اختلف في أنه له يجوز تسمية الله تعالى بما ثبت بخبر الواحدة فقيل: يجوز لأنه من باب العمل وقيل لا يجوز لأنه من باب الاعتقاد ثم الظاهر أن الإطلاق وإن كان جه الإخبار يكفي في الأذن ما لم يكن في السوق أمر آخر يمنعه وإلا يلزم أن لا يكون كثير من الأسماء مأذونة فلا عبرة بما في شرح المشارق من أنه لا يلزم من قول عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها أن الله رفيق يحب الرفق صحة أن يقال : يا رفيق لأنه ذكر على وجه الإخبار . قوله: (فكالحنان) هو بالتشديد من الحنان بالتخفيف وهو الرحمة قال الله تعالى وحناناً من لدنا ﴾ [مريم : ۱۳] .

أبدلت الواو همزة كما قالوا في وساد أساد.
The Seventh Observation Post - Third Objective: Naming Allah with Names is Tawfiqi (Based on Revelation). As for in the Quran, such as al-Mawla, al-Nasir, al-Ghalib, al-Qahir, al-Qarib, al-Rabb, al-Nasir, al-A'la, al-Akram, Ahsan al-khaliqin, Arham al-rahimin, Dhi al-tawl, Dhi al-quwwa, Dhi al-ma'arij, and so forth. And as for in the Hadith, such as al-Hannan, al-Mannan. And it has been narrated in the narration of Ibn Majah names that are not in the famous narration, such as al-Tamm, al-Qadim, al-Witr, al-Shadid, al-Kafi, and others. Their enumeration (ihsa') is either preserving them, for it is only attained by repeating our total and counting them repeatedly, or controlling them by confining, counting, knowledge, faith, and fulfilling their rights. In summary, 'limited to enumeration' in hope of entering Paradise. So we say: 'Allah' is a name specific to His essence, not described by other than Him, i.e., not applied to others at all. And it is said: It is a frozen proper noun (علم جامد) with no derivation, and this is one of the two opinions of Khalil and Sibawayh, and narrated from Abu Hanifa, al-Shafi'i, Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi, and al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on them. And it is said: It is derived, and its original is al-Ilah, the hamza was deleted due to transmission and the lam was merged, and it is from alaha (with fatha on lam), i.e., to worship, and this is what is meant by His saying: 'when one worshipped'. And it is said: al-Ilah is taken from al-walah which is confusion, and the return of both is an added attribute (sifa idafiyya) which is that He is worshipped by creatures and chosen by intellects. And it is said: the meaning of al-Ilah is the one known by the prophets and awliya, and it is possible to enter but it is ambiguous not known specifically by the first, and the source of karamat is supplication with it specifically, as mentioned by Imam al-Ghazali in Sharh al-Asma' al-Husna. And the truth is that the disposal by the Greatest Name and by any name of the names of disposal is dependent on attaining suitability (munasaba) with its named and adopting His attributes, and merely mentioning the Greatest Name does not achieve suitability, just as mentioning the name of a sultan does not benefit unless there is a special suitability with him. O Allah, benefit us.
His saying: 'As for in the Hadith etc.' There is disagreement about whether it is permissible to name Allah with what is established by a solitary report (khabar al-wahid). It is said: It is permissible because it is a matter of action. And it is said: It is not permissible because it is a matter of belief. Then the apparent view is that the use, even if it is in the manner of informing, suffices for permission as long as there is no other matter that prevents it. Otherwise, it would necessitate that many names are not permitted. So no consideration is given to what is in Sharh al-Mashariq that it does not follow from the saying of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that Allah is kind and loves kindness, the correctness of saying 'O Kind One', because it was mentioned as information.
His saying: 'such as al-Hannan' is with tashdid from al-hannan with takhiff, which is mercy. Allah said: 'and kindness from Us' (19:13).
His saying: 'and this is one of the two opinions of Khalil and Sibawayh.' Ibn Jinni narrated that Sibawayh was seen in a dream and it was said: 'What did Allah do with you?' He said: 'Good.' And he mentioned a great miracle. It was said to him: 'How did you attain that?' He said: 'Because I said that Allah is the most known of known things.'
The name: His saying: 'and its original is al-Ilah' - it is possible that the definite article therein is from the narrated (al-mahki), which is the apparent from the words of the commentator, and it is possible that it is from the anecdote (al-hikaya), such that the original was indefinite. And each group has adopted [a view]. His saying: 'from al-walah' - so 'Ilaah' on both views is fu'ala in the sense of maf'ul, but on the second its original is 'walah', the waw was replaced by hamza as they said in 'wisad' to 'asadd'.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage defines the name 'Allah' and discusses whether it is a derived or frozen proper noun. It begins by stating the principle that divine names are tawfiqi (based on revelation). Then it gives examples of names in the Quran and Hadith, and also mentions names found in Ibn Majah's narration. It emphasizes the importance of enumerating (ihsa') the names. Then it focuses on the name 'Allah', defining it as a name specific to God's essence, not applied to others. It presents two main opinions: (1) it is a frozen proper noun (علم جامد) with no derivation, attributed to Khalil, Sibawayh, Abu Hanifa, al-Shafi'i, al-Khattabi, and al-Ghazali; (2) it is derived from al-Ilah, which itself comes from the root 'alaha' meaning to worship, or from 'walah' meaning confusion. The passage then discusses the meaning of 'Ilah' as an added attribute of being worshipped. It also includes a note from al-Ghazali about the Greatest Name and the need for spiritual suitability. The passage ends with a remark about Sibawayh's dream and a grammatical note on the derivation.

Key Terms
توفيقية
إحصاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think any name can be used for God as long as it is respectful.
Correction: The names of Allah must be based on revelation (tawfiqi). Humans cannot invent names for God.

Checkpoint
What does the principle of tawfiqi mean regarding the names of Allah?
A) They are derived from human reason.
B) They are based on revelation.
C) They are arbitrary.
D) They are only from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The names of Allah are revealed, not invented, and enumerating them with knowledge leads to Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by a name in your dua, do you pause to verify that this name is indeed among those He has revealed? If not, what does that reveal about your attitude toward His authority in describing Himself? Decide today to learn one new revealed name of Allah from this lesson and use it in your supplication with full awareness.
?
The passage teaches that enumerating Allah's names is not merely counting but also 'controlling' them through knowledge and faith. Are you content with just memorizing the names, or do you actively seek to embody their meanings in your life? Which name do you most need to manifest right now to draw closer to Paradise?
?
The source lists names from the Quran, well-known hadith, and even less common narrations like Ibn Majah. Do you have an appetite to know Allah through all His revealed names, or do you settle for the famous ones? What step will you take this week to deepen your knowledge of a name you rarely consider?

The Name 'Allah': Proper Noun or Derived?
Defines the name 'Allah' and presents the derivation debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly debate on whether 'Allah' is a derived name or a frozen proper noun.

Core Idea
The name 'Allah' is unique to God, but scholars disagreed on its linguistic origin.

This lesson focuses on the name 'Allah' itself. It first defines it as a name specific to God's essence, then presents two main opinions: that it is a frozen proper noun (علم جامد) with no derivation, and that it is derived from 'Ilah' which itself comes from the root meaning 'to worship' or 'confusion'.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Note that 'Allah' is defined as a name specific to God, not applied to others.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the two opinions: (1) it is a frozen proper noun, (2) it is derived from 'Ilah'.
Note the scholars who held the first opinion: Khalil, Sibawayh, Abu Hanifa, etc.
3
Understand the derivations
The derived opinion says the root is 'alaha' (to worship) or 'walah' (confusion). Understand both.
Both roots suggest an attribute of being worshipped.
